Blacklegs Modern Cyclopedia.
This is a handy encyclopedia in eight neat
volumes of workaday size. While not so
full or so up-to-date as the very expensive
publications of its class, it contains an
immense mass of varied information on
nearly all subjects of enquiry, and strikes a
happy medium between the vast encyclopedia
in 20 or 30 heavy tomes and the disappoint-
ing single-volume compendium.
Strongly-bound in cloth and well printed ;
the set of eight volumes for 505., packed or
posted anywhere in Australasia.
The Minerva Library.

of them illustrated ; and the cloth binding is
strong and serviceable. Posted for 2s 6d
each.

CLEVELAND STREET SYDNEY
Queen Victoria does not take B’s in gloves
(as alleged by an ill-informed newspaper), but
“large sixes.” The Duke of Buccleuch possesses
a silver, model of the Queen’s hand—lucky duke !
“■which will refute the libel in ages to come.
An eminently respectable Melb. publica
tion is set on Sundays by Seventh Day Adventist
compositors. Yet if Jos. Symes wanted to set
his Liberator on the Sabbath by atheist labor,
the whole right-thinking community would be up
in arms, and Jos. would be languishing in a
dungeon within 24 hours.
Apropos of the Wairarapa enquiry, a story
is told of a witness at a similar court of investiga-
tion. The Bench took a week to decide what
ought to have been done at this wreck. The
captain said, when he heard the finding : “Quito
right, gentlemen, only you have taken six days to
think it over. I had less than six minutes.”
There is a good deal of logic in this view of the
case. The average Marine Board would be con
siderably brightened up if it was taken out and
wrecked occasionally, so that it could also try its
hand at deciding in six minutes on the right course
to pursue.
FIFTY YEARS A FAVORITE.
Th» multiplication of cures for all sorts and con-
ditions of complaints during the last decade has
become so bewildering that many people have
sworn off what are called patent medicines alto-
gether. And we can scarcely wonder at it, for
there is a very large proportion of so-called medi-
cines which scarcely possess any known value in
the struggle against pain and disease. The
attention of. the public is therefore specially
directed to Pond’s Extract, as advertised elsewhere
in this journal. It is not a new remedy, for it
has been a popular favorite in the United States
of America for the last half-century—indeed, as a
positive specific for the worst cases of piles,
hemorrhages, and inflammations of every de-
scription, both internal and external, this famous
medicine, which is an extract of the well-known
Witch Hazel, has become a watchword to sufferers.
Pond’s Extract is being vigorously pushed in
Australia, and no doubt success will attend the
efforts of Messrs. M. Moss and do., the whole-
sale agents. for Australasia, of this renowned
medicine.

gin.

An Afghan post-mortemed at Ballarat
panned out an electro-plated table-knife, three
pieces of wood, tied together with a string, and a
piece of rag. When the doctors struck the table-
knife they began to enquire if deceased had ever
been an alderman.
Dear Bulletin,— Please inform “Usher”
that though the old Celtic Home Rule Club (to
which he probably refers) went into liquidation in
1891, the present Celtic Club, formed six months
afterwards, is solvent and lively, and gave J. F.
Hogan a splendid reception the other night.—
Yours, N. M. O’D., Melb.
In the William Manson alleged kidnapping
case the native potentate is again to the fore. It
is alleged in evidence that the chief Quisodia or
Imaoulid forcibly assisted the recruiters —in other
words, that he sold them suitable subjects on
reasonable terms. The voluntary Polynesian
again.
A certain Queensland M.L.O. was especially
wrathful, the other day, over the Payment of
Members’ Bill. Which brought to his enemies’
recollection the fact that he himself collected a
salary as M.L. A. as long as he could get it; also
a handsome screw as Mayor of a Northern city,
in which latter capacity be actually once made a
demand for overtime.
St. Patrick’s Cathedral Fair, Melbourne,
has realised a profit of <£10,000, but this is £10,000
short of the sum required by the trustees to make
matters easy. With two contracts on hand repre-
senting £40,000, it will require some extra
clever engineering and several more fairs to pull
the big building out of its financial mud. Those
people who, in boom times, promised to pay £20
to £100 yearly, are mostly broke nowadays, and
this is the kernel of the trouble.
Chairman of Committees Mason (Y.) con-
siders it smart to “ sit on” the veteran Longmore,
whenever thatdecent old man is more than ordinar-
ily emphatic. For instance, when Longmore said
that M‘Intyre, in the old days, was “ nothing but
a Freetrade barracker,” Mason, with some
acerbity, called him to order, and remarked that
“ the House was not used to that sort of language
now.” Truly the House that endured Bent and
which endures Patterson is growing particular in
its old age.

Dear Bulletin,— Re V. J. Daley’s note
upon the pronunciation of “Jaques” (the
“ melancholy ”), the orthoepy which rhymes the
word with “rakes” is the received stage-usage—
aa is also the English sound given to the “ch” in
“ Petruchio,” instead of the “ k”-sound proper to
it as an Italian word. Partially-informed persons
are fond of insisting upon the original pronuncia-
tion of those foreign proper names which have
acquired, by established English usage, an English
sound. The affected ass who inserts the French
“Pa-r-ree” into an English context is only a
shade more ridiculous than the cheap pedant who
carefully aspirates the first letter of “Juan,” or
calls the Knight of La Mancha “Don Keeghottay,”
and who —to be consistently Spanish—would have
to call the squire not “ Ganko Panza,” but
“Ganoho Pantha.” —A.M.

Dear Bulletin, —A larrikin told this story
in my hearing. He was drunk at the time. I
suppose the story was a lie, and I don’t even know
if it was an original lie, but I give it for what it
is worth. “You know Bill,” he said to another
larrikin, and the other one acquiesced. “You
know Bill? Well, Jim had the nark with him
over Liz. So we went w’ere Bill lives ’way up
Collin’wood. You know wot a fool Bill is—he
lives top of a pxace three storeys high. Boards.
Well, we sings out—‘Bill! Bill!’ And Bill, ’e
comes to the winder. ‘Bill,’ we sez, ‘Bill, the place
is afire!’ You know wot a fool Bill is. Bill sez
‘I’ll come down the stairs.’ ‘No,’ we sez, ‘they’re
all afire, too, you’ll have to jump—we’ve got a
blanket.’ You know wot a fool Bill is—well, ’e
jumped. Of course we never ’ad no blanket. You
should ‘ave seen Bill.” Then the pair laughed
wildly and departed.—Yours, Fluffy.

which you may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province, you can
h ive The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The'Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with ivhom use do not wish to
i iterfere.

to be addressed to William Maoleod, Managing Director.

Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
“ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

SATURDAY, DECEMBER 29, 1894.
Victoria and New South Wales:
A Comparison.
Ever since the long-enduring “fiscal ques-
t on ’ came to the front in New South Wales
it has been the fashion to draw parallels
between that province and Victoria in order
to demonstrate the relative advantages of
Foreign-trade or Protection, as the case may
be. The two states lie side by side ; they
are inhabited by people of the same race ;
they are much alike in climate and
population, and for these and other reasons
they lend themselves conveniently to this
species of argument. And the argument
lends itself so conveniently to all purposes
that each party has proved conclusively, over
Hid over again, by figures which cannot lie,
that its own theory is absolutely right and
the opposing theory wholly wrong. Some-
times the results are arrived at by a compari-
son of two isolated facts ; sometimes by
garbled statistics and the invention of evi-
dence ; generally by the foolish and illo-
gical assumption that all good results,
no matter of what kind or variety, are
attributable to “ our ” fiscal policy,
and all bad ones, from financial dis-
honesty down to the destruction of the
crops by caterpillars, to the other man’s
fiscal policy. This mode of reasoning never
reaches a definite conclusion, and leads
nowhere, and proves nothing ; as a means ,
of upholding any special faith it only serves
the same unscientific purpose as bad language
and road - metal. Nevertheless, there is
much valuable information to be gained by
a comparison of the relative situation ot the
tw» provinces, when it is made on the widest
possible basis, and The Bulletin proposes to
furnish such a comparison, and to make it as
comprehensive as the available figures will
admit. The usual custom is to seize upon
one isolated circumstance and to preach a
journalistic sermon thereon ; The Bulletin
proposes to supply instead all the pertinent
facts to be found in the published works of
Mr. Coghlan, the New South Wales Govern-
ment Statistician, and leave the sermon
mostly to inference. The result will be as
clear an exposition of the relative merits of
Foreign-trade and Protection as the unsatis-
factory science of statistics and the difficulty
of tracing cause and effect can permit.
# # #
The two provinces did not by any means
start abreast in the race—a fact that is
generally overlooked in instituting compari-
sons. New South Wales was in existence
for half-a-century before its rival was first
heard of. It had a considerable population,
a large export trade, and much accumulated
wealth before Victoria even began to come
into existence. It has also nearly four times
the area of its neighbor, a soil at
least equally rich, as evidenced by
its superior yield per acre in many
varieties of crops ; and an abundance
of coal, iron, and various other raw
materials essential to manufacturing in-
dustries, wherein Victoria is almost wholly
deficient. Except in the matter of gold, in
which it has long been pre-eminent, Victoria
comes far behind New South Wales in almost
all natural advantages. The Southern pro-
vince has produced, however, in the aggre-
gate, £232,000,000 worth of the yellow
metal, against barely £40,000,000 extracted
from the mines of New South Wales. How
much solid advantage was gained by this
circumstance no one can profess to know,
for the country was raided in the early
auriferous days by the adventurers of all the
earth, and the gold was dissipated to the
furthest ends of the globe. Victoria
kept the lead as a mineral-producing
province until seven years ago, when
its yield sank into comparative insignificance.
New South Wales now produces minerals
to more than twice the value of those
of Victoria ; consequently the latter
province has lost —and, to all appear-
ance, lost irrecoverably—the only natural
advantage which it ever possessed over
its neighbor. During the last seven
years, therefore, it is reasonable to
assume that New South Wales, with its
enormous area, its mineral production,
its huge wool export, which Victoria—for
want of space—cannot possibly rival, its vast
public lands revenue, which the adjacent
province, through lack of lands to produce
revenue, is utterly unable to approach, and
the alleged blessings of Foreign-trade in
addition, should have left the small, frag-
mentary southern state a very long distance
behind. It should have done that much
even without the advantages of Foreign-
trade, but with them thrown in it should
have done it much more definitely. If it
failed to gain any appreciable advantage,
then the inference is that the great Foreign-
trade policy is rather an incubus than an
aid.
Agriculture is admittedly the most impor-
tant of all industries, and in this branch Vic-
toria is a very long distance ahead. The
total value of all crops in 1893 was £6,665,000
against rather less than £4,000,000 in New
South Wales. In 1861 the two provinces stood
about on an equality, but with the advent of
Protection, Victoria began to move rapidly
ahead until it not only became self-support-
ing in most respects but was one of the great
sources for the supply of New South Wales,
and other provinces. It is now, despite its
small area, the principal exporter of food-
products in Australasia. Its production of
wheat is more than 2| times that of New
South Wales ; and this despite the fact that
it yields per acre only half as much as its
more fertile competitor. It produces nine
times as much oats, eight times as much
barley, nearly three times the quantity of
potatoes, twice as much hay, nearly twice as
much wine, 50 per cent, more fruit, and
nearly seven times as much in the shape of
minor crops. In the matter of tobacco,
maize, and sugar-cane, New South Wales has
a remarkable lead, principally, so far as the
two latter are concerned, because they refuse
to grow in the Victorian climate ; but, on the
whole, the larger and older province is vastly
inferior to its neighbor. And, when allowance
is made for the fact that the average produce
for each acre in New South Wales is valued
at £3 18s. against £2 19s. in Victoria, the
discrepancy is even more marked. The fact
that the Victorian farmer achieves such re-
sults on inferior soil says much in his
favor, or else in fafcdr of the system under
which he works. During the two years of
partial Protection under the Dibbs Adminis-
tration, New South Wales did a good deal
towards closing up the gap, which, in the
days of the old Foreign-trade regime, was
even more conspicuous that it now is.
* * #
In manufactures of all kinds, Victoria finds
employment for 167,000 people, and produ jes
£22,000,000 worth of manufactured goods
per annum ; New South Wales employs
140,000 persons, and turns out goods to the
annual value of £16,000,000. In this
respect the Southern province is grievously
handicapped by the fact that almost all the
coal employed in its industries has hitherto
been imported from New South Wales, and
the very small amount locally produced was
of inferior quality and extracted at heavy cost.
Victoria, unlike New South Wales, has also
no local supply of kerosene shale, and no known
iron deposits of any extent, and under
all these disadvantageous conditions its great
supremacy in manufactures is little short of
marvellous. Of the populations of the two
provinces, New South Wales has a larger
proportion of lawyers, civil servants, military
men, parsons, doctors, paupers, domestics,
habitual criminals, and various other varie-
ties of non-producers, but in the matter of
actual producers, despite her larger popula-
tion, she is considerably behind. And in
many other and diverse ways the progress
of Victoria is very marked. A much smaller
proportion of the population lives in one and
two-roomed shanties ; there are only 24,000
of these unwholesome habitations in the
province against 40,000 in New South Wales.
Victoria has by far the more extensive rail-
way system. Its illiterate population is very
much smaller than that of N.S. Wales. It has
60 per cent, less criminal convictions, and —
for the one tiling is to some extent connected
with the other—it has more than twice as
many free libraries ; also, it has 30 per cent,
fewer paupers. There are 28,300 small land-
holders—this term being used to cover
owners of holdings of not over 100 acres —in
Victoria, against 25,800 in New South Wales.
Also, Victoria has 21,000 holders of between
100 and 500 acres apiece, against only 17,000
in New South Wales. In the matter of
large estates, however, the latter has a bad
pre-eminence. Victoria is, to a great extent,
a province of small, independent cultivators;
New South Wales one of big landed mono-
polists. Victoria has more savings-bank
deposits and nearly twice as many depositors,
and it has more deposits in ordinary banks
and building societies. At the same time,
New South Wales has the questionable honor
of being considerably ahead in the matter of
loans on mortgages, and the Cornstalk
farmer, so far as the available figures show,
is much more heavily involved than the agri-
culturist across the Murray.
# # *
On the other side of the case, New South
Wales is generally estimated to possess much
the larger aggregation of private wealth.
Its surplus, however, consists wholly of land
values. Owing to its superior area it has in
private hands nearly twice the acreage of the
adjacent province, and this natural advantage
more than accounts for the difference.
Again, New South Wales is the largest ex-
porting province in Australasia, being
£7,000,000 annually ahead of Victoria, but
this surplus consists almost wholly of wool
and cattle, and its supremacy is again
entirely owing to its vast extent. By com-
parison with agriculture, wool-growing is a
pursuit which requires little labor, and here
the natural advantages of the older province
have compensated for the evils of its fiscal
policy and the unprogressive character of its
inhabitants. Also, its stupendous area has
blessed its Government with a revenue from
public lands which even now amounts
to £2,200,000 per annum. Against this
Victoria, by reason of its narrow
dimensions, can only show a total of
£500,000, and the difference of £1,700,000
yearly has enabled the Government at
Sydney to keep its finances moderately
straight, and to ascribe its partial solvency
to the blessings and advantages of Foreign-
trade. If it were possible, however, which,
at present, it is not, to put the two provinces
on a similar footing in this respect, the New
South Wales Treasury would be, so far as all
evidence goes, the more insolvent of the
two.
• • •
How much of the aggregate discrepancy
between the two provinces is to be ascribed
to fiscal systems, and how much to other
causes, no person can pretend to accurately
estimate. The raw process of logic which
ascribes all the difference to tariff policies is
obviously an absurdity ; it is too wide, and
covers too many varied circumstances to be
wholly owing to any one cause or set of
causes. But the figures supplied by the
highest statistical authority in New South
Wales, at all events utterly demolish the
favorite assumption that Freetrade neces-
sarily spells prosperity, and that Protection
is synonymous with ruin. They show con-
clusively that the Protectionist province,
with a vastly inferior area, a less fertile soil,
a smaller population, and a grievous lack of
almost all the mineral resources which are
essential to manufacturing pursuits, and
with a history shorter by half a century, has
somehow overtaken her Freetrade rival in
manufactures, agriculture, and, to put it
shortly, in everything except those few
pursuits which her climate and her want of
space make almost impossible. And if these
results are, as the Foreign - trade party
alleges, in no degree whatever owing to her
fiscal policy, then it rests with that party to
explain what they are owing to.
Some Australian Characteristics.
The ingenuous Annie Besant, before leaving
Australia, confided to an interviewer her
general sense of disappointment with the Aus-
tralian character. The Australians, it seems,
came to see Annie as they would have come to
see a fat woman, or a sea-serpent, or a two-
headed calf; and when their curiosity was
satisfied they simply went away again. They
did not stay to be converted to theosophy ; in
fact, they did not seem to care anything at all
about theosophy ; they paid their cash to hear a
clever woman speaking, a woman whose name
and reputation were familiar to them, and they
paid rather more readily when she did not
speak about theosophy, because they found
other subjects more interesting than that vague
and mystic muddle. When Annie waxed elo-
quent about the threefold plane and the seven-
fold path and the fifteenfold fiddlesticks, they
only smiled pleasantly and said “ Next!” From
which the lady argues that Australians are not
mentally alert. Only dull people, apparently,
do not swallow the awful crammers of theo-
sophy. It doesn’t seem to have occurred to
Annie that the fault might lie with theosophy.
# * #
In any case, as The Bulletin recently pointed
out, Sydney and Melbourne audiences are not
Australia. Intellectual life is, of course,
keenest in the cities; but its intensity there
lacks something of the breadth and strength
occasionally found in the isolation of the bush.
The divergence of type which Clarke antici-
pated between residents in the temperate zone
and residents in the tropics, is really growing,
as Adams noticed, between dwellers in the in-
terior and dwellers on the littoral. The dif-
ference in temperature between North and
South Australia is not sufficiently marked to
establish any vivid contrast of character. The
difference between the conditions''of bush and
city life is already showing itself in the mind
and physique of the people. The advantage
is with the bush. Stand in a Sydney or
Melbourne street on Saturday night and
note how large is the proportion of weedy,
stunted forms, and callous, degraded faces
in the passing crowd. The bush, giving
health and strength not to be found in
alleys and factories, dowers manhood and
womanhood with dignity. Bush monotony, it
is true, narrows all but strong and original
minds. The flatness of existence, the raiity
of elevating intercourse and rational recreation,
too often deaden bright spirits into soulless
apathy. The cities are precocious and vicious.
The bush is sometimes dull and apathetic. The
character of its denizens is colored by their
sombre environment. Theirs is a stoic philo-
sophy ; that of the coast is gay and epicurean.
Annie Besant’s city audiences laughed at, her
dogmas without taking the trouble to seriously
understand them. In the bush, people would
have wrestled with and overthrown them.
There is a note of feminine frivolity about
Australian cities; a tinge of masculine harsh-
ness about the Australian bush. The cities
judge intuitively; the bush reasons logically.
But conclusions respecting’theosophy would be
identical all over Australia.
« * «
And, despite Annie Besant, our careless and
slightly contemptuous attitude to wards her latest
foible shows the presence of “mental alertness”
rather than its absence. Theosophy, as a creed,
does not demand the intelligence which com-
prehends : it only asks the faith which
for granted. Had she come among us as a cfitic
instead of as an advocate, Annie Besant would
probably have found, as all other critics have
found, that intellectual brightness and agility is a
prominent Australian characteristic. Australian
smartness seems indeed united with shallowness.
This conclusion, reached by local observation, is
made reasonable by foreign experience, and sup-
ported, among other things, by the noticeable
merit of Australian journalism and light Austra-
lian literature, and the noticeable lack of literary
works involving severe study and long-con-
tinued effort. But there is no doubt about the
smartness. Schoolmasters know that Aus-
tralian children apprehend much more readily
than English children of the same age and class.
Climate —involving, as it does, speedier develop-
ment ; habitual association with elders; and
greater social comfort, are chiefly responsible
for the quickened wit.
* « «
It would be a good thing for Australians if
the average temperature of their continent
could be reduced 10 degrees all round. The
quick change from childhood to adult age
tends to weaken the national physique ; and
in the coastal districts, especially North of
Capricorn, the intense heat enervates. In the
interior, as Miss Shaw has noticed, the warm,
dry air has tonic properties not found else-
where. But Australian growth, generally
speaking, is too rapid to secure the finest type
of physique. We gain in height by losing
breadth of shoulder and valuable chest-space ;
and many parts of Australia lack the lime
wanted to make good bone. Yet the race now
being evolved will be fitter for its environment
than any which could be imported. The writer
has seen sun-dried Queenslanders and ruddy
Maorilanders trudging 200 miles to a Northern
rush. They started all together, side by side,
but on the third day many of the Maorilanders
—big, beefy, stalwart men—were being carried
by the tough, lathy Queenslanders. In feats
of strength the Australian bushman may easily
be beaten ; but for feats of endurance who can
surpass him ?
• # *
The rock of Australian irreverence, on which
Annie Besant split, is well-known to every
pastor and spiritual master who attempts to
sail over life’s solemn southern main. Among
Australians born there is no religion worth the
name. They are frank pagans, intent on
getting as much joy as possible in this world,
and altogether careless of the next. It is in-
teresting to watch the facile descent to Austra-
lian heathenism. Nearly all immigrants who
reach our shores make a point of going to
church twice every Sunday. Their children go
once—until they are married. Their grand-
children do not go at all. The parsonical occu-
pation is already falling into disrepute, and the
parsons are turning to trade, or education, or
politics, to make a living. It is quite con-
ceivable that the sixth generation of native
Australians will pay their threepences to see
“ That great curiosity—the Parson ” exhibited
as the tenth wonder of the world in a circus
menagerie. And this want of respect for
traditional fetishes is another proof that Aus-
tralians possess the “mental alertness” which
Annie Besant denies them. For only stupid
ignorance worships without knowing why.
“The intellect has no knees.” Australians
yield ready homage to pluck, to knowledge, to
genius. But they have a keen eye for counter-
feits, and every claimant for veneration has to
show a satisfactory title. The respectable Mr.
Kidd, in his recent book on “Social Evolu-
tion,” extols reverence as if it were a valuable
quality no matter what .its object. It is a
horrible thought to respectable people that the
world is beginning to enquire into the validity
of its ancient shibboleths. The Bulletin
glories in the indisputable fact that young
Australia doesn’t care a goburra for shibboleths ;
and that its attitude to the universe is not that


of a crouching suppliant, but of a man—“ erect
upon two legs ” after the fashion of the
“ being” who deceived Mrs. Bard ell.
• • •
That Australia’s insouciant reception of theo-
sophy has also met many things of much
more importance native observers are painfully
aware. Our half-gods have gone, and our gods
have not arrived. The national character
wants deepening and strengthening in the stress
of national need. Compare the interest in
Federation with the interest in an international
cricket-match. Nobody but a rare thinker, an
infrequent editor, or an occasional politician
goes out of his way to do a hand’s turn for
Federation. The people are vaguely acquies-
cent ; they believe that Australian union is
necessary and desirable; but they are not
enthusiastic about it. They do not sit up
nights brooding over the subject; and meet-
ings to discuss it can only get a quorum
in the cities. But the man in the street
crowds all day to gaze at the scores
of the cricket-match ; and thousands of young
Australians, well-born, well-fed, well-clad, with
exactly the same kind of countenance covered
by exactly the same kind of straw-hat, pay
eagerly to witness the play; while the news-
papers are forced to give cricket as much pro-
minence as they give Parliament. There is no
objection; cricket is a good thing; yet one
would like to see equal anxiety shown for better
things—things not of passing local interest, but
of permanent national importance. There seems
ground for the fear that the making of the Aus-
tralian race is proceeding under too favourable
conditions. It may be that in order to realise
all the hopes which our poets have uttered for
us, we shall yet need to pass through the fur-
nace of suffering, and serve our apprenticeship
to sorrow.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Imported Militaire.
There was no mistaking the temper in which
New South Wales Assembly attacked the mili-
tary estimates. By 58 votes to 18, £7OOO was
knocked off the total, with the understanding
that it was to come from the “allowances ” for
mythical houses or fabulous horses or fairy-tale
forage by which many officers substantially
increase their pay at the expense of the peren-
nially-bleeding country. By 50 votes to 31 the
item of £26,000 for a holiday encampment was
struck out altogether. The Bulletin cordially
approves. The province is well able to save
£33,000, and to spare its value in imported
militarism. This paper has no ambition to see
established here the army of idlers which every
European nation scarves and sweats to maintain
—a military caste contemptuous of the toilers
who are taxed to feed it, and at all times ready
to aid in extinguishing popular liberties. The
New South Wales commandant naturally
magnifies his own calling, and there is plenty
of floating sentiment to agree with him. The
red-coated popinjay who forms the average
woman’s type of valour is admired also by
effeminate men, fascinated by fife and drum,
glow and glitter. But no sensible man, no
really courageous man, ever thinks of the
soldier at his best as other than a necessary
evil. In Australia he is unnecessary. If there
is one thing above all others which the demo-
cracy of this Commonwealth should sedulously
combat, it is the establishment of a standing
army on the lines advocated by Major-General
Hutton— to tax the earnings of industry, to
clog the feet of progress, and to set the
organised drones of clique and privilege above
the honest manhood of independent labor. The
chief present risk of Australia lies in her con-
nection with Great Britain, which at any time
may expose her to attack by Great Britain’s
European enemies. That connection once
severed,, there is little to fear; since we have
room and welcome for men of every nationality
whom we can meet upon an equal plane of
civilisation. If there be risk, it is chiefly a sea
risk, which can best be met by the establishment
of an Australian navy, manned by Australian
seamen, in place of the half-dozen leaky-boilered
vessels armed by Britain, manned by Britain,
officered by Britain, and commanded by a
British admiral over whom we have no control
whatever. If there be need of land defence, the
whole male population should be drilled and
trained to the use of arms ; but the function of
the professional soldier must be confined to
advice and direction. The present system is
an expensive play, of which the encampment is
an extra - expensive appurtenance. Did an
enemy once land in force Major-General Hut-
ton’s half-trained levies would meet the familiar
fate of raw recruits opposed to veterans. Aus-
tralia should be defended at sea rather than on
land. In any case, the creation under the
Southern Cross of a hireling army which places
the citizens who support it under the domina-
tion of an idle military caste, with the object of
perpetuating the nightmare of war which is
rousing tortured European serfs to revolt, is a
thing abominable, intolerable, and monstrous.
♦
Opportune Resentment.
The Victorian Legislative Council, at time of
writing, is getting ready to resent Turner’s
taxation-bill as a gross personal insult. The
Council’s resentment was a foregone conclusion
from the first, so the “ insult ” comes in handy,
as it were. Not that the outrage upon the
Council’s dignity could have been avoided by
any means. Some years ago, when the cloud-
speck of direct taxation was barely visible afar
off, Mr. F. Sargood took precautions to make
this political affront inevitable. He claimed
for the Council a right to amend money-bills
sent along from the Assembly, and James Ser-
vice, with one cold, damp optic gazing steadily
at the distance, winked his other eye at the
new religion, which was well calculated to meet
a crisis like the present. Sargood’s ingeni-
ous claim has no constitutional leg to
stand upon, but an influential section of
the Council decided to nurse it until
such time as it would serve them for a griev-
ance. The hour for brandishing this abortional
“right” came the other day when the taxa
tion bill was submitted to the Council for
acceptance or rejection—but not for amendment.
Just previously, Sargood, together with an
unportfolioed minion named Pratt, had thrown
off his ghost of a disguise and retired from the
Ministry. This was the anxiously-awaited cue
for Council indignation. The “ insult ” was at
hand. The little mob of “ old politicians,”
elected by landowners for landowners, no
longer railed against the principles of the bill.
Some of them even pretended a mild affec-
tion for the article itself. The “arrogant”
manner in which it had been sent up
by the Assembly was the only thing that hurt
their feelings. President Zeal, for instance,
moaned as follows to a newspaper interviewer :
“Such little amendments as we might have
made would not have affected the purpose of
the Bill, and now I do not see how the Council
can avoid taking action to resent the course the
Government has adopted. They must resent
it.” The President of the Victorian Legislative
Council appears to gauge popular intelligence
by the puerile pomposity of his political class.
The Turner Government adopted the same
course with the taxation Bill that has ever been
followed in cases where money is concerned.
The “ insult ” which the Council couldn’t avoid
resenting is merely an impudent fiction, con-
ceived by a swaggering Tory. Victoria at
large is the affronted party, and the country
should at once consider the possibility of in-
dicting Sargood, Service on a charge
of criminal “ cheek.”
A Roaring Farce.
Another humorous feature crept into the
Federal Bank case, last week. From time to
time since the breakdown of that shocking
institution, there have been disturbances among
the ruins, and on each of these occasions
“something” was vaguely supposed to be on
the point of going to be done in the matter.
Long years ago, stormy meetings of share-
holders and depositors were held, whereat the
voice of Fatman, crying “Shame!” upon the
victims, was powerless to suppress the utter-
ance of many unkind remarks. Subsequently
the story of the Munro clan enjoyed a large
circulation. The fact that the Federal Bank
had been more or less devoured by a group of
unfortunate speculators became unofficially
known to everybody. After another long
pause the question of ousting ex-manager
Priestly from his position as liquidator was
submitted to a Victorian J udge, and then, for
the first time, the eccentricities of the Munro
clan were officially observed and commented
upon. The Judge talked freely of what
might happen if certain people’s conduct
were under consideration in another place, but
other judges had said much about the same
thing concerning other unfortunate speculators
who never reached the place where they might
have been, and once more the matter seemed to
drop with a dull thud. This application to
remove the old clanny liquidator certainly did
yield a practical result, for Priestly lost his
desire to guard the assets, and quietly de-
parted for England. Considering, however,
that Priestly had already rubbed a lot of the
clan’s liability off the slate, and could make
very little difference to the bank’s welfare two
and a half years after the failure, it was
hard to see a reason for his removal, unless it
was to clear the way for doing the something that
never comes off. The application re Priestly
was spread over many months, and an enormous
amount of costs came out of the bankrupt
estate in consequence. A few more months
rolled on. The Federal Bank case got into the
newspapers again, and the lawyers were found
to be extracting a living wage from an examina-
tion of William M‘Lean, an ex-director. Here
was a chance to bring more facts to light at the
poor bank’s expense. An application was made
to a Judge, who ordered that William
M'Lean should give evidence before the
Insolvency Court. The Federal Bank has
not filed a schedule; seemingly, therefore, a
special license for M‘ Lean’s examination was
necessary. After the witness had proceeded a
little way with his amusing disclosures, and
bursts of forgetfulness, he was pressed to un-
ravel the mystery of a certain £25,000 cheque
paid to him on account of the Vic. Baptists’
Fund. On this point William M'Lean de-
clined to be questioned, protesting that Baptist
Funders are “bound to secrecy.” Half-a-dozen
excited reporters hereupon inked their pens
anew in anticipation of “spicy” copy. Then
Mr. M‘Lean’s legal protector came to the
rescue with a really brilliant objection. It had
just struck him that the Insolvency Judge was
not entitled to continue the examination in
public. He argued that his Honor was “ not
sitting in open court,” and a learned discussion
ended in the Judge “ treating the court as his
chambers,” and ordering press and public to get
out. The latest phase of the Federal Bank case
remains shrouded in gloom. The public will
hear no more about the Baptist Fund, or Mr.
M‘Lean’s examination, The Bulletin' sup-
poses. In this instance the groaning creditors
of the Federal must pay another colossal bill of
costs, without getting any fun for their money.
♦
The return laid before N.S.W. Assembly by
Premier Reid as to the Civil Service retrench-
ment of his hated predecessor, has been so
utterly discredited that it has at last been
withdrawn by its authors and suppressed.
According to this publication, Hie Dibbs
Government, during its term of office, “ re-
trenched ” 372 public officers and appointed
1582, the assumption being that, while preach-
ing economy, it added no less than 1200
new officials to the national pay-sheet. The
return was got up in curious fashion. On one side
it accounted for the civil servants who were dis-
charged from the service through retrenchment
only, and took no notice of those who died, or
resigned, or were discharged for other reasons
than retrenchment, or of temporary officers—
such as the 300 or so engaged making out
electoral rights and doing other work under the
E ■ Loral Act—who were dispensed with in the
n i ural course of events, when their temporary
duties were finished. On the other side were
included all appointments, temporary and per-
manent, including navvies, telegraph messen-
gers, and everybody else that could possibly be
included. When one clerk left his billet in a
public office, and seven subordinates got each a
step up in consequence, they were returned as
seven new appointments, and when some
petty functionary was shifted from one town to
another he turned up in the document in like
manner as an entirely new functionary. So did
the man who was shifted to replace him, and
the one who was shifted to replace him, and so
on. Premier Reid, in withdrawing his mis-
leading publication, professes to have presented
it to Parliament without examination, and in
blank ignorance of its character. Seeing that
it was Reid himself who originally moved for
the return, and that it was prepared by his own
subordinates for his own benefit and to his own
order, this utter indifference is remarkable.
Dear Bulletin,— i?e“ that registration clause,”
I would remind you that in Texas they have
what they call a homestead law, which appears
to pretty well crystallise your opinions on this
subject. The law, as quoted in Knox and
Sweet’s “ On a Mexican Mustang,” runs as
follows :
Article 2335 of the Revised Statutes of the State of
Texas provides that the following property shall be re-
served to every family exempt from attachment or execu-
tion, and every other species of forced sale for the pay-
ment of debts, except as hereafter provided : —(1) The
homestead of a family. (2) Household and kitchen furni-
ture. (3) Any lot or lots in a cemetery for purpose of
sepulture. (4) All implements of husbandry. (5) All tools,
apparatus, and books belonging to any profession
or trade. (6) The family library, portraits and pictures.
(7) Five milch cows and their calves. (8) Two yoke of work-
oxen, with necessary yokes and chains. (9) Two horses
and one wagon. (10) One carriage or buggy. (11) One
gun. (12) Twenty hogs. (13) Twenty head of sheep. (14)
All saddles, bridles and harness necessary for use of family.
(15) All provisions and forage on hand for home consump-
tion ; and (16) All current wages for personal service.
Article 2336 interprets “ Homestead” of a family not in
town or city to consist of 200 acres, with improvements.
“Homestead” in a town or city means a lot or lots used
as a home, with its improvements to a certain value.
This seems to be direct enough, but there must
be considerable difference of opinion as to the
advantages of such legislation. It appears to
be fatal to the credit system, and a poor man,
it seems to me, would be very likely to remain
poor under its fostering care. Indeed, under
such a law it is difficult to see how selectors in
this province (Victoria) could have got on at all
with the storekeeper and banker turning a deaf
ear to them. I have known selectors without
capital round about this locality—the few good
men with credit from the storekeeper—to come
out eventually in a good position, and the lazy
or incompetent and inexperienced majority in
the same position to go under as soon as their
holdings became alienable. They all had their
chances, good and bad, and the good men sur-
vived. Under a homestead law none of such
men would have had a chance at all. What do
you think? —Yours, S.C. Euroa, Vic.
Judge a’Beckett (Vic.) had a spasm of virtu®
the other day. “The way in which country
administration suits are worked,” he said, “is
enough to make the law stink in the nostrils of
the public—
Litigation frequently arises out of the administra-
tion of comparatively small estates, and, as the country
solicitor employs an agent in town, and this agent in turn
employs counsel to appear in court, the bill of costs swells
rapidly. It is a perfect disgrace to the law to see how
some of these estates are squandered. One is almost
ashamed of the law that such things should be possible.
A hundred pounds is sometimes spent to ascertain a lia-
bility of £5.”
Consider the various aspects of this feeble
whoop. The system is a disgrace to the law,
and makes the law stink in the nostrils of the
public ; but Judge a’Beckett is only “ almost”
—not quite—ashamed of the law in conse-
quence. The law has to stink an awful lot
before a judge gets quite ashamed of it.
Thus the annual report of the “ National
Scripture Education League,” Vic. :
The correspondence between the Rev. S. Savage and
the Government statist had shown that secular education
in Victoria had been followed by a large increase in
criminality, especially amo.ig the native-born population.
The average religious publication is brother to
the weather-report and next-of-kin to the liver-
specific “ad.,” and in this case it lives up to
its usual character. As a matter of fact, the
correspondence of the Rev. Savage— whoever
he may chance to be—can’t show the alleged
increase of criminality, for no such increase
exists. Since secular education began in
Victoria, crime, as shown by the police records,
has diminished in proportion to population, by
about one-fourth. There is certainly more
crime among the native-born population than
there was, but that is solely because the native-
born population has increased ; in proportion
to the number of adult natives criminality has
decreased enormously. During 1881, out of
every 10,000 adult Victorians, 550 were
arrested for various offences ; in 1891, only 396.
It may further interest the Scripture Education
League to know that in Westralia, .the only
province where education is still conducted on
a Scriptural basis, there is three times as much
serious crime, and more than twice as much
crime of all descriptions, as in the “godless”
states of Australasia. The Scripture League is
either shamefully ignorant, or else its disregard
for accuracy is its phenomenal as that of its own
Scriptures.
Sky-pilot after sky-pilot, in and around Syd"
ney, from a Romish Cardinal down to the Shep-
herd of a non-hierarchical conventicle, has
scoffed at Spiritualism, now that its local
phosphorescence has been out-glittered by the
vermilion tlames of a public conjuror. Which
is, to say the least of it, curious and consistent.
Scoffing conies naturally to the rhinoceros-hided
heart of the materialist, in whom want of faith
is logical; but for the religionist to scoff is
most inconsequential. The chief corner-stones
of each religion are—miracles. Spiritualism is
founded on—miracles. Spiritualism is a religion,
too, of a nondescript sort. Therefore, when
the Shepherds of the Unknown scoff at
Spiritualism, it is only one superstition heaving
mud at another. The Spiritualists are a
sorry lot in argument. Their stock defence
seems to be that Spook-manipulator Hosking,
who claims to be merely a wicked conjuror and
hireling magician, is really a “ meejurn,” selling
his Christ for pieces of silver. Were the
writer a spiritualist he would adopt another
tack. Why not bring up the fabulous stories
of Moses, class Hosking as an Egyptian
magician, and prophesy that the genuine
Aaron’s rod, metamorphosed into a serpent,-
will swallow the Egyptian’s counterfeit every
time? Or, refer to Simon Magus, and claim
the orthodox Christians as spiritualistic advo-
cates. Put the Witch of Endor into the
witness-box, and call Cardinal Moran to give
her a bad character. Under the analysis
of bold, bad scepticism, conventional spiritual-
ism and religionism must live or expire
together.

The Bulletin last week advised Premier Reid
to make an end of New South Wales Legisla-
tive Council’s obstruction to progressive legis-
lation, and if necessary to make an end of the
Council as at present constituted. This week
it repeats the advice with all the added
emphasis justified by the Council’s latest freak
—-the mutilation of the Appropriation Bill,
Opinions may easily differ as to the policy of
the Reid Cabinet in replacing upon its esti-
mates the sum of £216,000 which the Dibbs
Cabinet “saved” from roads and bridges
expenditure. But the validity or otherwise of
that policy is not the point at issue. The
question is simply : Are the people of New
South Wales to be governed by their
representatives, fresh from a general election?
or are they to be governed by Messrs. C. G.
Heydon, Watson, Kerr, Pigott, Charles,
John Davies, Hoskins, Hill, R. H. Roberts,
Moses, Dangar, Cox and Dr. Maclaurin?
Such a question answers itself. When The
Bulletin went to press the action to be taken by
Government had not been fully decided. Upon
the heads of the Ministry lies the responsibility
of vindicating the cardinal principles of demo-
cratic Government, at whatever cost to the
Chamber which shelters the unlucky 13
nominees.
The S.A. Adult Suffrage Bill, which allows all
women over 21 years to vote without any pro-
perty-qualifications, passed its third reading by
31 votes to 14, although the second reading was
only carried by one vote. The passage of the
measure is a great victory for the Govt, and the
Labor-party, especially as the omission of the
property-qualification clauses gives the demo-
cratic-party vastly increased power at the«pol-
ling-booths for all the city constituencies. Op-
positionist Downer and his magnificent retinue
of seven vainly stonewalled at all stages, and
were badly beaten on the property-qualification.
The fact of such a measure passing through
the Council simply illustrates the wonder-
ful alteration that has been worked in that
formerly Tory chamber. Three years ago a
Woman’s Suffrage Bill was contemptuously
rejected by the senile councillors even though
there was a £5O property qualification tacked
on to it, but the Chamber has since been in-
vaded by six direct labour members and four
democrats, and the Tory element has actually
less influence there than in the Assembly. The
measure seems fated to pass. The third reading
in the Council, which had to be carried by an
absolute majority, was postponed for weeks
owing to the illness of labor member Guthrie,
and in the Assembly on two occasions the
Ministerialists stonewalled their own measure
te prevent a division in the absence of the
required number of members. However, S.A.
democrats have secured their toy, and it will bo
interesting to see what they will do with it
Gilbert Casey appeals for public subscriptions
to pay the passages to Paraguay of those
starving Rue Australians who have already
paid the Association for their passages, but
who can’t be sent to their ghastly destination
because the Association has muddled away the
money for other purposes, and thus betrayed
the trust which was placed in it. Mr. Casey
speaks as follows
This appeal is issued upon my own responsibility, in
order that I may sm ceed in the task I have undertaken.
To do that I must have the assistance of outsiders just
now ; because our association has been left absolutely
stone-broke; because it has a number of unfortunate
women and children left on its hands, whose husbands
and fathers have been in Paraguay for the past 12 months;
because it is cruelty to let this continue any longer
through the fear of being sneered at by those who mis-
understand or have no sympathy with the movement ;
because many of our members gave up all their
possessions to the association officers, left their
employment as instructed, and have been in desti-
tute circumstances ever since ; because these unfor-
tunates cannot tie expecled to do a great deal towards
assisting others, though they are doing their level best;
because there is no hope of the association getting any-
thing o it of the Tar or other property ; because it is better
to abandon the attempt than fool round with the law ;
because it is right to send these people across; because
£5O w ill send them all to their husnands and fathers ; be-
cause, finally, a small sum from each of our friends will do
what is needed, and my trust in men and women is great
enough to make me feel absolutely certain that this money
will be received b fore the end of the first week in
January.
A strange situation. From Buenos Ayres the
British Consul appeals to the Australian Govern-
ments—so far, The Bulletin regrets to notice,
without effect—for money to bring a lot of
starving “ mates ” back to Australia. In Aus-
tralia, Gilbert Casey appeals for money to
take another lot of starving mates out to the
land of hunger which the first lot is striving to
get away from. One thing is certain : Any
money subset', bed for the poor Rue Australians
should not be handed to’, or in any way be con-
trolled by, the sorry set of messers through
whose instrumentality the catastrophe has been
brought about.
The retirement of Frederick Sargood,
Victorian Minister of Defence, is the luckiest
tliintr wiiioli lias yet happened to the Turner
Government. It was impossible under any cir-
cumstances to take the bellicose draper seriously.
He was eaten up by a perpetual yearning to rush
out of his ribbon department and mobilise some-
body, or declare war, or throw up entrenchments,
or do something equally ridiculous. He was so
full of vast, incomprehensible, military manoeuvres
that he had no time left for ordinary business, and
his craving to form the Cabinet into a hollow
square with artillery at each corner, or to induce
the Education Department to fix bayonets and
charge, made serious deliberation impossible.
Also his maunderings over commissariat and
cannon and such like things were painful to wit-
ness. He parted from his colleagues ostensibly
over some small and hazy matter connected with
the Land-tax, and in the case of any other equally
high-dried Tory the explanation would have been
sufficiently plausible. In Sargood’s case it is
only possible to assume that he was dissatisfied
with the military policy of the Government, and
felt aggrieved because hostilities hadn’t yet broken
out. Therefore he resigned and went home, pre-
sumably to fortify his drapery - emporium and
prepare for the very worst.

846 Maoquaris-street North, Sydney,
December 25, 1894.
My Dear Moorabinda—
“His Excellency ” Vice-Admiral Bowden-Smith
and his genial admiraless are billed to leave Aus-
tralia per the Ormuz on 14th prox., much to the
grief off Sydney society. Admiralty House has been
a perfect little social heaven during the Bowden-
Smith reign—indeed, the naval (and military) hospi-
talities have more than atoned for the somewhat
infrequent festivities at the legitimate social
apex, Guvmentouse. In recognition, then, of the
heroic way the Bowden-Smiths tugged in the
social collar during their term of office, a public
banquet is to be hurled at the Admiral in the
Centennial Hall, on Wednesday, the 9th prox.,
and a reception to the popular Mrs. Bowden-
Smith will take place at the Town Hall the same
evening. All the three-tailad Bashaws of Sydney
society fig ire on the committee-list, and Messrs.
A. W. Gillies and E. K. Cox are the hon. secs.,
either at the Town Hall or 39 Hunter-street.
Rear-Admiral Bridge (his front appellative is
“ Cyprian ” —what a lovely name to murmur in
the moonlight!) is thj next person to pose as our
naval figurehead, and protect Earm Cove at the
peril of his life. As he is billed to arrive some
days before the present people leave, etiquette
will pack him away somewhere on the Orlando,
even if it’s only to bucket about outside the
Heads until the Ormuz comes down stream. It
would never do to have two mackerel—l mean,
admirals—in the bay; nautical etiquette couldn’t
stand the strain. There would be far too much
“ Get up Jack, let John sit down” about the
situation to make it possible even in a nightmare.
The new admiral is alleged to be rather a poky
sort of entity who (having probably heard all
about the big hide-store erected cheek-by-jowl
with his_ official residence at Kirribilli Point—and
no special grant for lavender-water, either)’has
expressed his intention of living on the flag-
ship, so that if society wants to scare him into
giving tea-parties, it will have to climb over guns
to get at him. There is no Admiraless —a state of
affairs that society rejoices at or deplores accord-
ing to whether it has unmarried daughters or not.
The last Admiral who came out unattached mar-
ried a Melbourne girl, you remember.
Admiral Bridge’s suite possesses a somewhat
Australian interest, Mr. B. Hopkins (no relation
to The Bulletin's Hop., mind you), who comes
au the gallant tar’s secretary, is closely con-
nected by marriage with a Melbourne family
known to fame as mine hosts of one of Mel
bourne’s swellest hotels. This same youth, by the
way, took in all his friends this side with a hoax
made familiar in London by the Gaiety Co., but
quite new to Australia. He was the proud father
of a baby girl, and sent out full particulars : “ She
is a very pretty little thing,” he wrote, in a solemn
strain, “ but unfortunately she hasn’t all her
fingers on one hand.” And it was only when
people had got ever their preliminary shudder
of pity for the poor little thing that they saw
through it. Admiral Bridge’s young man is
evidently a jokist of the deepest dye.
Talking about the Queen’s navee, I am quit®
sure that not one person in a hundred who prowls
over a spruce warship and admires the lavish
trimmings the'reof gives credit where credit is due
for all this burnishing of brass and dolling-up.
The Admiralty isn’t responsible for all the gor-
geousness—it only allows for black and white
paint in scanty quantities, and all the valentine
effects come out of the commander’s fob. If Cap-
tain Corcoran wants to run a ship one mass of
gold-leaf over and beyond the reg’lation coatings
he pays for his little vanity just as the humble
land-lubber pays for a fresh coat of pea-green
paint on the yard-pump.
Miss Melbourne Punch Murphy writes cor-
reotingly to the Bulletin in re a recent “Con-
fidence ” to the effect that the patrons d’honneur
(Lord and Lady Hopetoun) figuring in her sub-
scription-list are only a French version of the good
old charity-ball “ patrons ” who put their hands
in everybody’s pocket but their own. As a matter
of fact, Lord Hopetoun has not only lent his
moral support to the forthcoming literary venture,
but has bespoken a number of copies. Nobody
but a person who can’t see a toad without wanting
to advise him to pawn the jewel in his head in
order to subscribe to a South Sea mission would
expect the complaisant Victorian potentate to do
more.
And talking of novels reminds me that one bud-
ding authoress (not to put too fine a point on it)
was advised, on good authority, to get the names
(merely) of all the Australian Governors on her
subscription-list, as the illustrious roll-call would
have a paralysing effect on the grovelling soul of
the British publisher-man, and probably lead to
more gilt on the cover at reduced rates. All their
Antipodean Excellencies (bar one) gamely respon-
ded at the first call, and not only lent their valu-
able signatures, but ordered copies of. the book
into the bargain. Sir Robert Duff alone stood
out. Although he was not petitioned to subscribe,
only to “ lend his signature ” —“ R. Duff, his X
mark,” would have done at a pinch—he declined
to lend his name, and superfluously refused his
subscription, although nobody asked him for it 1
But a Scotchman can’t help being canny all
round.
By the way, it may be a tip to coming literary
genius (“ within the colony ”) to know that His
Ex. does not give his name and subscription to
books “ published outside the colony ” —which
appears to mean that His Excellency blandly
the priceless boon to books published
within it. And (another clever pointer) quite the
latest Guvmentouse epistolatory mannerism is
to call a lady “Sarah Blank” without the prefix
inside. I must say that it looks horribly inelegant,
but I suppose it’s Prince of Wales-y.
Williamson and Musgrove must be warmly
congratulated by all right-thinking people in
having staged the most sartorially prim panto-
mime at Sydney Lyceum that I have ever sat out.
What “ Cinderella ” principally suffers from is a
great rush of clothing to the figure. Imagine a
fairy godmother who sticks to her quilted red
satin petticoat, high grey bodice and long grey
hat all through the piece and never once lets a pale
pinky silk-stockinged leg shoot out through a
crack in her discreet draperies as a guarantee of
good faith ! Miss Ray Jones (the rather attractive
lady looks as if her surname were Cohen in private
life), an ex-amateur from Melbourne, played
Cinderella, who appeared for the first time in
theatrical history as a brunette. Miss Jones was
not unduly prominent in the title-rdle, and I can-
not help thinking that the cropper she came on her
first entrance (making her bow to a Sydney
audience in the first swimming position) may be
regarded allegorically as affecting .her stage-future,
since “the profession” is so incurably super-
stitious.
Miss Jones’ toilets were not of the happiest
description. Cinderella’s butter-color ball-gown
had a distressing reach-merdown air about it, and
her white wig fitted her cranium about as neatly
as a hen covers a china nest-egg—in fact, Miss
Jones, from being a promising amateur, has
become a very unpromising professional, to my
idea. When Miss Courtenay, in her capacity of
fairy queen, remarked that “A booky she will
want,”it really did seem the one thing needful, for
nobody but a seasoned metallician could precisely
tell what were the exact odds on Miss Jones becom-
ing a public favorite. Another disappointing thing,
from the point of view of the front stalls, lay in
the fact that Prince Peerless was a short, aggres-
sive he-importation from ’Ome, supported on a
most commonplace pair of legs, instead of the
orthodox she-prince, in to-be-continued stockings,
who is much more satisfactory at pantomime
time, when good old grandpa makes a solemn
point of taking the children.
I liked' the procession of sports, and it was
interesting to see that the very first pastime
to get a hand was boating. By the way, the
behavior of the “ boys ” and girls taking part
in these pageants threw a strong light on the
sporting female, who evidently isn’t kept
amused by the game alone, for whenever (in the
pantomime) boating men parted from boating
girls or lawn-tennis hes from net and racquet shes
—in fact, in all the sports where women figured,
the lads and lasses never separated without kis-
sing ! But this, I hope, is a stage fiction, for I
am very reluctant to believe that the lawn-tennis
girl or the yachteress would ever countenance
such frivolous goings-on.
It was also profoundly interesting to observe
that the sports which the Lyceum manage-
ment presumed that women weren’t mixed
up in, included billiards (which every second
Melbourne women plays now as well as a
sharper!), horse-racing, football, cricket and
lacrosse, and the only pastime from which the
noble male was excluded was bathing. This un-
pardonable oversight was probably due to innate
modesty on the part of the management, who did
not think the abbreviated trunks in which man
takes to the water quite complete enough for stage
representation in a censorious world. There isn’t
too much striped paint and gold-leaf about
“ Cinder,” and the neck-to-knee way in which the
fairies are dressed looked as if the N.S.W. ex-
Attorney-General had been fixing his baleful eye
on Titania’s dressmakers. One thing is certain :
the Firm didn’t smother ’em all in clothes for fear
of shocking the new Attorney-General, “ Jack ”
Want. But perhaps it was done in deference to
—all hail!—Mr. Justice Simpson. I am sure it
would be easy to shock Mr. Justice Simpson.
I see that “colored teas k I’Americaine ” are to
take the place this summer of the colored lun-
cheons of last years, and of the two I think I
prefer the teas, for men go to the teas, whereas
the lunches were women-only affairs. A Sydney
matron gave a pink tea the other day. Every-
thing was the hue of the parboiled shrimp, and
most of the guests sported pink in great quanti-
ties—save one. He was (I suppose I ought to say
is) a well-known Sydney man, and when he
appeared in the drawing-room in ordinary morn-
ing costume and perceived the glare in the eye of
his hostess he meekly drew the attention of the
company to the fact that he had pink-silk socks
op. I hear that a blue tea is being talked about.
Are the little stories to be en suite?
Colored teas have one great attraction from a
strictly feminine point of view—there is no better
way for a blonde to “ kill ” a brunette rival than
to give a pea-green tea and watch her victim
rivalling the sunshiny hues of a pound of butter.
Then the brunette effusively retaliates with a
scarlet tea, at which the blonde looks like an over-
boiled potato. It’s a merry game, if you enter
into it scientifically.
On dit that a very distinguished lady who was
recently provoked into handing out the prizes at a
Southern sectarian school, did it with such bad
grace that it completely spoilt the philanthropic
effect. Carpet was laid down for her at the rail-
way station under dozens of triumphal arches, but
she refused to walk on it, perambulating gingerly
along one side, although the committee implored
her to walk freely on the pattern. Then, although
hordes of infants had been readied up to sing
melodies elaborately suited to the occasion after
the prizes had been given, the lady simply poked
the literature on to the model scholars and bolted
in hot haste. I believe the committee is still
gaping with astonishment.
Melbourne Confidences.
Melbourne, December 17.
Yarmouth, the terpsiehorean earl, broods over
the irreverence of the local press, and declares
that, were the Victorians to ask him on their
bended knees, they should never behold him in
skirts. But, perhaps, some of his implacability is
due to the coldness of our sovereign lord and lady.
The Hopetouns, misled by “unsympathetic re-
ports,” are said, so far, to have completely ignored
the poor innocent’s existence.
Probably this isn’t the Governor’s fault, for he
himself slithers on an extra-smooth board and
gambols boyishly even in the State Lancers; but
one can’t answer for her ladyship, who treads a
measure with the demeanor of Marie Antoinette
going to execution, and curtseys to the very
ground a,s if for the express purpose of extorting
apoplectic reprisals from stout, short-necked part-
ners.
“Yarmouth” must by now deeply regret his
historic Hobart leg-show ; anyhow, he fairly
snorts with anger when asked again to frill and
exhibit his noble calves in the sacred cause of
charity. The misguided young blood probably
now discerns his true position in the stage-world,
which closely approximates that occupied in the
realms of sawdust and spangles by the double-
headed calf and the oyclopean goat. There is, I
am told, much doubt in Hobart elderly-spinster
circles whether Heaven will quietly accept the
charity-fund which the Earl raised by showing his
blue-blooded legs, and a meeting will be held to
prayerfully consider the question.
The coldness between Yarmouth and vice-
regality seems confined to our parish, for the
N.S. W. Governor is apparently on excellent terms
with the Earl. As for Gov. Gormanston ; he took
young “Bloaters” to his heart at first sight, and
the Earl’s Hobart skirt-dancing was patronised on
both nights by the Tasmanian vice-regal party.
Lord Gormanston is an indulgent patriarch with
an amiable weakness for private theatricals.
I bear that the Tasmanian belle, who acted with
the English peer in “ A Pair of Lunatics,” is, as
a rule, handicapped by Pa’s rooted objection to
the stage, but when it comes to obliging Her
Majesty’s representative, a loyal subject will
sacrifice even his beauteous chee-ild upon the altar
of Duty. ~
The sudden stoppage of the Yarmouth revels
has extinguished some bright hopes, which already
began to irradiate the gloom left hanging over
society by a recent fashionable alliance. Feminine
mourners fall back once more on their old sources
of consolation, viz., the bridegroom’s comparative
impecuniosity and the color of his hair.
Some people say Lord Yarmouth isn’t half so
offended by those newspaper remarks as he pro-
fesses to be. Like all the rest of the notabilities
he abuses the press in public and pastes all the
cuttings about himself in his album in private.
“I was standing beside him at a Melb. exhibi-
tion of dancing,” writes a friend, “and over-
heard him relate with great gusto and laughter
some of the paragraphs and verse he stigmatised
as ‘ disgusting.’ His Nibs, who doesn’t look more
than 18 at most, doesn’t seem half a bad sort of
fellow, and appears to have a far greater allowance
of brains fhan the average alleged aristocrat.”
The Austral Salon lately had the honor of
entertaining the seraphic Percy Granger, a boy
of undoubted'musical genius, whom they should
have “ discovered ” a few months ago. This little
lad, with his wistful blue eyes, waving golden
hair, refined features, and delicate complexion, is
a bright exception to the rule that talent and
ugliness go together, the one unpoetic feature
about Percy being his low - comedy walk.
He rolls from the hips like a ship at sea.
It was in connection with the financial side of a
contemplated European training that Percy’s
visit to the Salon had been arranged. The pros,
and cons, of the undertaking were explained at
length to a sympathetic audience by quaint old
Baron Von Mueller, whose full dress for once
lacked the time-honored woollen comforter in
which it is the owner’s custom to beg the pleasure
of a dance.

widower, blessed with ample means and pretty
daughters, who are themselves shortly to be
married. “ Taking on ”in the usual style of step-
children elect, they decline to hallow the union by
a filial blessing.

merrily until the happy pair had left for their
honeymoon and the guests were gone. Then, to
the surprise of her remaining offspring, the new-
made mother-in-law gave way to tears and

had sailed by the Wairarapa, and goodness only

One fair Tasmanienne was surprised at seeing
her name among the superscriptions of a Melb.
journal’s Oup illustrations. Innocent even of a
dream of the races, Imr inclusion in the group
aroused in her high-toned home-staying elan an
uneasy suspicion, to be allayed only by Time’s
soothing touch.
Probably the mistake arose from a commission
given by the damsel in question to a Melb. cos-
tumi&re in anticipation of the Hobart Exhibition
festivities. Which suggests that perpetrators of
“ Carnival studies,” so popular of late, require no
knack of singling out the successes of Flemington
fashion-displays, but rather an intimate acquaint-
ance with the order-books of popular modistes.
The Hopetouns still rusticate at Wiridgil, the
Countess apparently not having quite recovered
from her alarming encounter with Wilfred, the
dog—an anarchistic tyke which bit her knee the
other day.
Just now the course of religion runs unusually
smooth at Camperdown. Sectarian jealousies have
temporarily vanished, the church lambs browsing
amicably in the thorny dogmatic pastures of the
kirk, and the kirk kye lying down in peace beside
the slumbrously still waters of the church.
This brief armistice is due to the example of the
Wiridgil inmates, who patronise each establish-
ment in turn, their orisons being shared by wor-
shippers of all creeds and conditions in such
numbers that the other tabernacle (whichever it
happens to be) is for the time quite deserted.
The anniversary of Carlyle’s birth afforded the
Salon an excuse for an especially gay gathering,
though, as a matter of fact, the Bruin Sage did
not begin his troubles until the following day.
But that’s where womanly ingenuity comes in.
The hostess, anticipating history to suit her own
convenience, climaxed the moveable feast with an
ice-cream refection. It was a little difficult to see
the connection until it was explained in a crisp
paper, read during the afternoon, that the Hero
of the day had been a life-long martyr to “ bilious
dyspepsia.” A comedietta from the pen of a
local editress, which enlivened the Carlyle ffite,
was laughable enough, promising well for the
brightness of the authoress’ just-out book of short
tales.
A certain Australian University professor last
year gave an order for certain essays of Addison’s
to be included in the matriculation text-book for
1895, under the impression that the edition he
named was an expurgated one. Now that the
book arrives, it is discovered that the professor’s
memory had failed him—the edition is an un-
Bowdlerised one. An attempt was made to with-
hold its publication, but as six months will elapse
ere another book can be prepared (that University’s
text-books, it would seem, have to be printed in
Hingland) the volumes must go out with all the
attractive passages left in for the wicked boys to
grin at and the girls to blush over during dinner-
hour.
Mrs. Silas Harding has purchased (for, it is said,
.£13,000) Bracknell, the palatial late Toorak resi-
dence of Matthew Davies. It was mortgaged for
<£20,000, and cost at least .£40,000. The lady was
saleswoman at Melb. Exhibition, and, at its close,
visited Geelong as traveller in diamond jewellery.
She there met Silas Harding, a rich, old and
eccentric squatter, who married her, and at his
death left her residuary-legatee to the tune of
about half-a-million, untrammelled by any restric-
tions. As a wife, even censorious and envious
Geelong cannot detect a flaw in Mrs. Harding.
Her story is a true romance.

suppress those coupon competitions which an-
nounce a Xmas gift upon condition that a penny
stamp is enclosed with the coupon. A sporting
print gave a tenner on the Melb. Oup in this way

strong at the funeral of M. Poutiata, the Russian

potatoes are being hawked in Melbourne at Is.

A Victorian jury has returned a verdict of
“not guilty” in the case of Jane Chrosier, the
girl who gave such a frank and thrilling account
of how she, at the instigation of the family
generally, split the skull of her sister’s illegitimate
infant with a spade, whereupon its brains were
eaten by hens. The court’s decision was that
Jane’s confession was only a hysterical romance,
and that she wanted to be hanged and to get her
portrait in the evening papers out of a certain
Deadwood Dick species of cussedness. The young
lady should make a soulful wife for somebody
when she grows up.
Every day now the cable informs us that
one or other of the London papers— i.e., one of
the staff on one or other of the London papers—
approves or disapproves of such things as the
Wairarapa verdict, or the test-match team of
cricketers, or Sid. Gregory’s innings, or something
else on which a bumptious English opinion,
uttered in utter ignorance of the facts, can have
no value whatever. And the mulish dailies print
these opinions in large type, and bow down to
them, and write leading articles to show their
errors, as if anybody in Australia cared two
snakes about them. The process of freeing our-
selves from the Drivel of our Fathers is very slow
and painful.

The Wild-Cat Column .
The auctioning of bank lands —payment taken
in deposit-receipts—has met with success in Melbourne.
Non-depositors are how being invited to compete in these
auctions. Their promissory-notes will be accepted, to be
afterwards redeemed by deposit-receipts at their face
value. This is a very good scheme for the depositor, but
the non-depositor needs to be careful in entering into
such a bargain. Everything is possible—even a corner in
deposit-receipts.
