The new name for the Australasian Rights Pur-
chase Co. is the “Electric Supply Co. of N.S.W., Ltd.”
Last week they advertised their intention of again apply-
ing to Parliament for leave to bring in, &c., &o. Some
anxiety is being displayed by certain M.’sP. lest they
should be asked to father the bill now that the redoubt-
able late member for Bingara is politically no more.
* * * ♦
What a comment on “reconstruction” is the
clause in Premier Turner’s Ineoine-tax Bill, entitling a
taxable subject to deduct from his or her income the calls
paid during the year to liquidating companies and banks
“ the shares of which have no market-value 1” Consider-
ing that such calls are supposed to represent so much
additional interest in a sound and prosperous business,
the audacity of the clause was magnificent* It called
upon Parliament to admit that certain banks have no
chance of living, and that every penny paid on account of
ordinary shares is lost to the payer. Parliament admitted
it without hesitation.
» » * *
The determination to reduce the interest on
deposits in N.S.W. Government Savings Banks to 3 per
cent, on deposits at call, and 4 per cent, on those fixed for
twelve months, practically makes the savings of the work-
ing classes worth only 3 per cent. Not one person
in a thousand of this class is able to give a deposit for
twelve months in advance. Savings-bank history is, in
the main, a story of lives of putting in and taking out, of
climbing up and sliding back. Only the classes for which
savings banks were never intended can take advantage of
the 4 per cent.
* * ♦ *
At a meeting of the Federal Building Society
(Melb.), the other day, an artless shareholder stated that
he made a point of attending auction-sales of the Society’s
little properties for the purpose of “bidding up.” He
advised all friends of the good cause to encourage the
real estate market by bidding up in moderation.
* » * *
The Bulletin keeps on badly wanting to know
why the Australian hanks are not required to keep at
least half their reserve-funds in Australian 3J per cent.
Government securities. It would be a guarantee that so
much, at all events, of the reserve really existed, that it
c uld be found when wanted, and that it was earning
r asonable interest ; and it would enable the State to
raise more money locally with which to pay off the
B itish creditor as he falls due. Most other countries
compel their financial institutions to do something of the
kind. Even British consols keep up their value mostly
because trustees and others are compelled by law to invest
in them, and it is mainly owing to this forced circulation
that J. Bull now borrows money at 2J per cent.
» ♦ * *
It is announced that the National Fire Insur-
ance Co.—a good-sized and hitherto prosperous concern—
has been bought up by the London and Lancashire. Thus
another Australian office goes out of the business, and an
E iglish institution absorbs its connection. This process
of annexation is going on so steadily that the English
e mpanies seem likely, by-and-bye, to have a monopoly
of the business in Australia, and meanwhile the export of
1 rofits to pay divs. to English shareholders proceeds
aily. This utterly useless leakage of Australian money
to England is only a comparatively small item among
many, but still it represents a continuous drain for which
this country receives absolutely nothing in return. When
will some Protectionist Government live up to its prin-
ciples by enaoling that no fire insurarce co. that hasn’t
its headquarters in Australasia shall he permitted to do
business in this country? And why shouldn’t Govern-
ment start a fire-office? If it can successfully run a
colossal life-office, as in Maoriland, why should it not run
a colossal fire-office ?
* * * •
The Australian Widows’ Fund is rousing its
members to agitate for the exemption of mutual life
a-suranoe societies from the land-tax. The plea is that a
life soe’eties’ lands and mortgages are the property of
hundreds of individual members, and in most cases each
man’s share is so small as to fall within the exemption,
limit. The plea has some show of reason as a matter ot
abstract justice, but the bottom falls out ot it as a matter
of expediency, and politics, at the best, are mainly a
matter of expediency. So many building societies and'
hanks and other corporations would get through on the
s.me plea that there would be very little land-tax left.
Nobody ever yet fixed up a tax which would absolutely
square with the doctrines of abstract justice, and nobody
ever will.
♦ * * •
A person calling himself “Septuagenarian”
loiters into the columns of the Melb. dailies, bemoaning
the lot of the. widows and the aged who may he called
upon to supply income-tax under the new Act. They
will have to pay double, says the alleged ancient, because
they derive their incomes from interest, instead of per-
sonal exertion, and the effect of this inequality will be
their immediate departure for foreign lands. Considering
that the widow and the aged are to enjoy the same
amount of exemption as a personally-exerting victim
who has a wife and 15 children to support, the moan of
the geyser falls rather flat. Yet it is a significant fact
that the editors who print his letter forget to give him
ids due in a footnote. A widow with, say, £5OO a year,
who fled the country to escape Turner’s income-tax,
would be taking a long journey on very slight provoca-
tion.
♦ * * •
A scheme for hurrying up the slow and melan-
choly liquidator is on hand in Victoria. As matters stand,
the liquidator of a big concern is often paid by salary,
therefore it is to his’ interest to keep on liquidating. Or
else he is paid by a percentage on receipts, and then it is
his interest to spend £lOO in law expenses to recover a dis-
puted debt of £2O. Also, he can’t he made to give any
definite information about how things are going on
except by an order of the court, and it costs often £3O or
£4O or more, in legal expenses, to get the order.

A few years ago, when the Melbourne boom
was at its height, the office of the boss boomer took fire.
An excited pressman announced next day that £7,000,000
worth of securities were on the premises and narrowly
escaped combustion. Now you might light a twopenny
cigar with that £7,000,000 worth of securities, and no
one would object.
* ♦ • •
Here is an interesting fact for statesmen to con-
sider: A country which borrows money abroad and has
to export the interest can only raise the said interest by
selling produce in a foreign market—in fact, it pays, to
all' intents and purposes, in produce. But prices of Aus-
tralian produce have diminished so greatly in recent
years that the burden of interest on the N.S. Wales loans
floated between 1872 and 1891 has already increased, in
effect, from £1,600,000 to £2,300,000 annually. Also the
loans were received mostly in English produce ac high
prices in days gone by, and they will have repaid
if they ever are repaid—by the sale of Australian produce
at low prices in the future, so that the effect will be the
same as if the prices had remained unchanged, and Aus-
tralia had to pay £l5O for every £lOO borrowed. By-and-
bve as the drop goes on, the effect will be the same as if
this country had to pay £2OO, or even £3OO, for every
£lOO borrowed. It is impossible to count the number of
different ways in which the borrowing curse comes home
to roost. . „
* * * •
The Victorian Government thinks of enacting—
if its good intentions are not overset by the Legislative
Council—that in future the local banks should he required
to show live and dead business separately in their balance-
sheets. The former coasists principally of properties
which have been taken over, often at a loss, in satisfac-
tion of overdue advances, and these have hit lu rto been
included in the bewildering item of “Bills discounted
and all other debts due to the bank ” —which item
generally forms nine-tenths of the total assets. The
proposal is at least a move in the right direction. A lot
of light would be thrown on sundry mysterious institu-
tions if they were compelled to show separately: (l)The
nominal value of town and city properties actually on
hand; (2) the value of country properties on hand; (3)
the amount of bills under discount; (4) the amount of
loans on live mortgages ; (5) the amount of interest over-
drffe ; (6) the amount of interest accrued ; (7) the amount
of advances on miscellaneous securities; and (8) the
amount of miscellaneous securities taken over through
non-payment of advances. Probably there will be several
more panics before legislation reaches this point, but it
will arrive there, or somewhere thereabouts, sooner or
later.
* ♦ ♦ ♦
Lendrin Economist suggests that “ British
holders of colonial stock should appoint a committee to
advise colonial Governments when desirable.” The
fatherly J. Bull always wants to advise somebody, but in
this case the offer won’t he accepted. The only thing that
a committee would be likely to advise is that “ colonial
Governments ” should pay up regularly, and look
pleasant, and shouldn’t attempt to float loans in Aus-
tralia on any account, hut should put them all on the
London market at 4 per shent. Finance is practically a
fight to a finish between the borrower and the lender, and
the Economist’s suggestion is very like Corbett forming
himself into a committee to advise Fitzsimmons when
their much-advertised “scrap ” comes off.

Mrs. Unwin, who died the other day in a
western Sydney suburb, was the widow of the holder of
the first policy which became a claim upon the funds of
the A.M.P. Her husband was one of the first hundred
members, and his policy contained the curious (as it seems
to people of the present day) provision that the contract
should be void “ if the assured were killed from casualty
by collision with the aboriginal natives of any part of New
Holland, or in consequence of injuries sustained in such
collision.” Things have changed a good deal since then.

British Guianan gold-yields are big enough to
deserve more attention. Mining is almost entirely in the
hands of the nigs. With the crudest methods in the
world, and the least brains, the province yielded
110,5560 z. in 1891-2, 134,1240 z. in 1892-3, and 142,0()0oz. in
1893-4. These yields would make an unspeakable sensa-
tion in Westralia.

A land-sale, advertised in Melb., brings to mind
a boom romance. A worried clerk was saddled, for long
and weary years, with a stretch of remote and unsaleable
suburban land which produced nothing but tax»S. In
the boom its sale swamped him with money, about
£15,000. He went to Paris with his pile and died there,
mainly of joy. The land is now worse and more unsaleable
than ever it was.

The partners in one of the largest Melbourne
firms of solicitors declared the other day to a Sydney
visitor that they hadn’t seen a conveyance for three years
—had “ forgotten all about them,” in fact. Their staff of
a score of clerks was occupied almost entirely in bank-
ruptcy business.
* * * *
Moule, M.L.A., Bent’s successor, violently
opposes the land-tax. Mr; Moule gets many briefs from
the Danks, whose solicitor (in about five instances) his
brother is ; besides, the brother is also solicitor for Money
Miller’s family and a crowd of absentee owners and capi-
talists. In fact, Moule, the barrister’s, and Moule, the
solicitor’s, interests are so bound up with the banks,
capitalists, and large land-owners that it would be busi-
ness suicide for the M.P. to advocate a land-tax. You
can’t nowadays expect a man to absolutely immolate him-
self Oh the altar of his busted country.

* ♦ * * i
Australian contractors urge the appointment of
a Prothonotary, with a knowledge of current building-
prices, to tax their final bills on contracts. This, they
explain, would save the architect the trouble of pro-
pounding his usual question—“ What aril I to get out of
this?” when the final account is tendered. The architect
at present constitutes himself the sole arbitrator on the
question of costs for extras, &c., by the insertion of a
cunningly-devised clause, making his decision final and
binding on the contractor, who, no matter how obviously
one-sided the architect’s decision may be, has to submit
rather than enter into a dreary and ruinously expensive
law-suit which, moreover, damages him in the eyes of all
other architects. No architect takes kindly to a fighting
contractor.
♦ * *
You can build cheaply in N.S.W. just now. A
Sydneyite called for tenders for the brickwork (labour
only) of a two-storeyed, eight-room house to be erected in a
railway suburb. The “successful” tender was that of
two working bricklayers, who contracted to do it for £l4.
It took them eight weeks, and they had to employ a hod-
man 1 Tenders for the plumbing, plastering, and wood-
work were almost on the same starvation-scale. And the
other day the writer saw the accepted tender for a country
homestead —a 16-room wood cottage built in Queen
Anne style of the best materials, 70 miles from Sydney,
and 20 miles from a railway. The contractor had to
supply all the materials and pay trainage and cartage.
Amount of tender, £450 1
♦ * * ♦
The “Unclaimed Moneys Act,” which was
passed by S. A. Parliament three years ago, has just begun
to operate. Under this law the banks are forced to hand
over to the Treasury all balances remaining urolaimed
after three years, and the Govt, on receipt publishes full
details. The result is that many widows and relatives
have recovered money of the existence of which they
never had—and, under the old system, never would have
had—any knowledge. Apparently, in former years, when
a depositor died leaving a balance, the banks made no
enquiry as to his heirs, but simply collared the money
wherever possible in approved banking style. No wonder
the financial magnates objected to the new law.

A recent Melbourne insolvent set down as the
cause of bankruptcy, " Owning 2000 shares in the Com-
mercial Bank of Australia.” Four years ago that meant
owning £27,000. Now it means owing £12,000. Things
are not what they seem.
♦ * • *
A sudden and violent drop_ in National Bank
ordinary shares, which have fallen in a few days from 15s.
to “sellers, 85.,” and no buyers, is another solemn con-
sideration. The National is one of the few reconstructed
banks whose ordinary shares always had a selling value,
and is undoubtedly the very best of the Melbourne re-
constructs.” The sudden caving-in is, therefore, all the
more depressing.
» • * *
A young bank-clerk, gaoled a while back for
fraud, although, at the trial, it seemed evident to many
that he was only the dupe of the real defaulter, the bank-
manager, and had never benefited by or stolen one penny
of the missing money, is entering an action for malicious
prosecution against the bank, claiming large damages.
The manager, who, at the time of the trial, took the
whole blame of the affair —along with the money stolen—
to himself, has been out of prison for some months, and
is said to promise interesting revelations at the trial of
the action.

As I mentioned a week or two back, writes The
Bulletin’s London correspondent, Malcolm Fraser, the
Westralian Agent-General, was weak enough to listen to
the voice of the promoter and allow his name to appear
on the directorate of a mining company which is to be
brought out. He soon discovered his mistake, but it was
too late, and the clever engineers refused to receive his
resignation until after the company had gone to allot-
ment, on the ground that his withdrawal would seriously
prejudice the company in the eyes of the publio. Mal-
colm, at latest, was trying to injunct the company and to
have his name removed from the prospectus.

A Coolgardie miner who applied recently for
the forfeiture of an uuworked lease, complying with all
due formalities in the Warden’s Court, saw his case
knocked kite-high by the production of a telegram from
Premier John Forrest, saying, “I personally granted
exemption (hree months ago.” When the Premier of a
province trumps his trick, the wretched, low-down W.A.
prospector can only throw up his cards and go and cool
his head in the nearest soak.
* * * •
The famous Hampton Plains (W.A.) estate,
which the Forrest Government sold to a British shindykit
tor £27,000, has just been traded off by the buyers to
another British syndicate for £300,000. This second gang
of brig mds will probably cut it up and sell it back to the
miserable Australians for half-a-million or so, and then
this country will be £470,000 out on the transaction. And
still this ruinous process of land-jobbing is described as
“ bringing capital into the country.”
* * * *
Out of the <£90,000 for which the Empress of
Coolgardie was floated, £30,000 stuck to the London
underwriters.
The Emerald, regarded as the principal mine of
Yalgoo, consists of a deposit of quartz on the surface. It
is a sort of basin in diorite. It is very rich, but the
deposit is limited to 200 feet in length, 75 feet in width,
and 10 feet in depth. Shafts have been sunk in the
diorite, bub no trace of a fissure can be found. There is a
five-slainp mill on the mine, and the ore in sight is under
1000 tons. The Bill Brouther is a mine 9 miles east of
Yalgo. It has a number of reefs of low-grade quartz ore,
with a short shoot of very rich rock.
* * ♦ *
Returned Coolgardians speak of their home of
dust and meat-tins as they find it. J. J. Scott, a Never-
tire (N.S.W.) prospector, who is returning East after two
years’ tramping, called in to tell Tire Bulletin that he
has no reason to complain anyway. His pet idea is that
the heavy gold has yet to be found far east from Kur-
nalpi. But he confirms the judgment concerning the
patchiness of the field which so many good critics have
already come to, and warns the public “ for God’s sake to
keep off the boom reefs.”
* • ♦ «
Four Governors have an interest in the Black
Flag Proprietary syndicate, which owns what is described
by a correspondent as “the finest surface show” about
Coolgardie.
» • * •
Bayley’s Consols is a water-mine which makes a
good living by selling the precious fluid to the Bayley’s
Reward Co. And the cruel part of it is that the water is
really from the Reward mine, which drains down to the
lower level of the Consols and is pumped up again and
traded off to the original owners. Like a man paying
rent for the use of his own house.
* * * *
The lowest fee for a report on a feline W.A.
mine is £2so in London. They do ’em for five guineas
and three tins of preserved rabbits at Coolgardie.

Michael Thomas, of Bendigo, chairman of the
Bendigo Coolgardie Co., has returned from his visit to
Coolgardie, which he says possesses all the charac-
teristics of a great goldfield. Of the co.’s own particular
mine he says that, although he m< t nothing phenomenally
rich, he saw large masses of gold-bearing quartz through-
out, and believes them payable. The machinery will
be finished in a few weeks.
* * * *
John G. Dunn, of Wealth of Nations fame, is
at present visiting his people at Hillgrove, N.S.W. He
intends passing the summer in New England, and will
again journey Westward about March. He believes there
is still gold to be found in Westralia, and has been
retained by a solid syndicate to lead a well-equipped
prospecting party.

The Westralian boom still languishes in Lon-
don. Not one-tenth of the companies which have been
floated receive sufficient support to pay advertising ex-
penses, yet they keep on coming, and no end of pro-
perties are awaiting the issue of the prospeetus of the
Wealth of Nations, in the hope of its giving an impetus
to investment. There is no hope of the sharks being able
to unload their shares, for the moment they try it, down
go prices on the Stock Exchange. Out of about 30 com-
panies, the shares of which are dealt in, hut not quoted
officially, no less than 20 are at a discount, which is lively
for the original shareholders. It may interest Zeb. Lane
to know that the Great Boulder shares are down to 10s.
for the £1 seripl

Dear Bulletin,— Re those flooded-oufc mines at
Wyalong. The deepest shaft I know of is Taylor’s, down
about 210 ft. The greatest amount of water met with in
eight hours filled 21 buckets. Will your correspondent,
who fears wp will all be drowned, please mention those
claims that have been flooded out ? Taylor’s reef is bigger
at bottom level than anywhere else. Your prophet gives
Wyalong 12 months to live. By that time the field will
just be weaned and ready to walk. Outside capital is not
holding the place up, anyhow. Everybody came here
stone broke, and the trouble now is that people can t get
stone broke fast enough. Batteries not ready and not
big enough. Stone now being taken to batteries at
Yalgogrin, Barmedman, Teniora and Sydney.—Yours,
John Smixhurst.

The New Zealand Loan and Mercantile Agency
Co. announces that it has, in its Sydney warehouses,
abundant space for the storage of wheat and other pro-
duce, and farmers desirous of sending their new crops to
Sydney for sale are invited to entrust their interests to
this old-established institution. Prompt and liberal
advances will be made, when desired, on the security of
produce forwarded for sale.

* * * ♦
Mt. Allen is an enigma. When it appears to
have plenty of stone and gold it has no water, and vvhen
there is abundance of water it seems to have very little
gold. Like nine-tenths of the N.S.W. gold-mines, it is
erratic, and, therefore, unreliable.
* • * *
Some attention is directed to the auriferous
drifts in Tasmania, where they are beginning to talk of
the potentialities of hydraulic sluicing. The country has
the advantage of abundance of water and fall, but it costs
more money to carry out the work of extensive hydraulic
sluicing than the average Tasmanian dreams of.
* * ♦ *
Swamp Oak (N.S.W.) “is looking up.” The
Highland Mary has crushed 80 tons for 1600 z., and diggers
who have been to Wyalong have returned to Swamp Oak.
Which is a very unkind cut for Wyalong.

From the quaintly-named locality of Snow-
ball {near Jinden, N.S.W.) comes the story
(related by “a well-known truthful boy,” named
“Johnnie M‘Williams ”) of a “wild man, or
gorilla,” having been encountered. The animal
bolted up a range, tearing up dust with its claws,
bellowing like a bull whenever it struck its feet in
vaulting over logs.
Parry-Okeden, magistrate in charge of the
disturbed districts under Q. Coercion Act, refuses
to endorse the squatters’ version of the late strike.
Okeden’s tongue is tied by his Govt, office, and
he can’t say as much as he would like; but he
does go so far. as to say that the men “ were not
altogether in the wrong,” and that “ there is a lob
to be said for therouseabonts in raising the pro'test
they did against the reduction of an already
miserable screw.” Considering the source of this
opinion, the strikers need ask for little further
justification.
Xmas holidays smash weekly-newspaper
arrangements. The Bulletin, for* example, is
generally printed on Wednesday and sent
round the earth for sale on Thursday; but
this week Tuesday and Wednesday are post-
official dies non , and the authorities are pledged
nob to handle any mail matter beyond a limited
morning delivery. The consequence is that The
Bulletin has practically to be made two days in
advance of its usual time. This explanation will
account for any want of up-to-dateness observ-
able.
Daring a private colloquy in the S. A.
Assembly on the occasion of one of the all-night
sittings at the close of the session, Premier King-
ston called Opposition-leader Downer “a damned
humbug.” Grainger overheard the remark and
reported it to the Speaker, his excuse being that in
September, 1884, Playford, then head of the
Ministry in which Kingston was Attorney-
General, reported a similar conversational remark
made by him to a brother member under like
circumstances. It is not often that even political
grudges are nursed so long as that. Grainger,
who seldom remembers a good turn, has, however,
an everlasting menaory for injuries.

The late eminent specialist being asked h«w he
accounted for the Small number of suecessful enres
in throat diseases, gave the following answer :
“ Our patients are, in most cases, under the in-
fluence of an irresistible habit, against whicti
advice is of no avail. The first rule for a diseased
throat is to abstain from smoking during the early
period of lha treatment; gradually, as the patient
improves, I allow him a very mild whiff until he
has completely recovered. But, what happens?
No sooner is he free from tire acute pain tlwn he
lights his old pipe, causing the essence of concen-
trated juice to come in contact with his still
delicate throa’t, and resulting in a new and more
dangerous inflammation.” Frossnrd’s Cavour
Cigars just answer the purpose. Being mild and
aromatic, they are harmless and give pleasure,
while the price of a packet of eight, only one
shilling, puts them within the range of all.

Robert Louis Stevenson once said he felt
a spasm of regret when he did not die at 37. “It
is the fatal year of genius. I knew then I wasn’t
a genius.”

The work on which Robert Louis
Stevenson was engaged at the time of
his death was “ The Lord Justice Clerk ”
—a special study of th,e times and career
of the ingenuous and brutal Scotch
Judge, Braxfield. It was on his last
visit to Sydney that Stevenson deter-
mined on the prosecution of this work,
the idea being suggested to him by the
coincidence that the room in which he
was conversing with a Sydneyite was
built upon the site of Maurice Mar-
garet’s cottage. Margaret was one of
the famous body of Scottish martyrs—
Muir, Skirving, Fysshe Palmer, Gerrald
and Margaret—who were transported by
Braxfield for “seditious practices.”
S.A, Govt, gets another probate-duty
windfall through the" death of Dr. Mayo, the
wonderful old man who raced Adelaide streets on
a bicycle till within a few weeks' of his death, at
the age of 88.
Ernest Hiscock, one of S.A.’s most pro-
mising cricketers, died the other day, aged 26.
He was a very promising batsman, and performed
well against Grace’s team, three years ago.
Young Hiscock was possessed of much literary
power, and gave up a good position to work at a
poor salary for S.A. Land-tax party.

The ancient Cassidy, who lately died in
Yass (N.S. W.) district, had been drawing a pen-
sion since 1827 for being shot by a bushranger.
The story goes that another ex-constable, who
lately died after long drawing a similar pension,
obtained it for “plugging” a ’ranger with slugs
after the outlaw had died from a shot from his
own pistol.
W. W. Tarleton, the long, gaunt, un-
happy-looking barrister who died in Sydney the
other day, came of a well-known Tasmanian
family, and was originally an Anglican clergy-
man, but went over to Rome a score of years ago.
He was not very successful at the bar, but he did
his level best, and was of so amiable a nature
that the news of his death was received with
genuine regret.
Frank Iredale, N.S.W. batsman, whose
portrait is printed in this week’s Bulletin, first
played cricket in earnest with the Balmains.
Afterwards hedrifted into theßelvideres,of which
club he was long a prominent member; he now
plays with the Central Cumberland electorate.
Most followers of cricket thought he should have
been included in the last team which toured Eng-
land, but the selectors opined differently, giving
as their reason that Iredale was not strong
enough to stand the racket of an English trip.
Iredale is a sure catch, an especially good
fieldsman in the “out-field,” and Ids consistent
scoring with the bat has shown that his many
admirers were right in their contention that he
was worthy a place in any Australian representa-
tive team. In his first big match Iredale made 5 7
against Victoria, and after scoring consistently
ever since he has placed the hall-mark upon his
cricketing career in the last two matches. Play-
ing for N.S. Wales against the Englishmen, he
made 133 in an innings ; and last week he put
together 81 in his first innings in the test-match.
Messrs. Blackio and Sons, of Glasgow, the
well-known publishers of boys’ books, will shortly
issue a book of Australian adventure by Ernest
Favenc ; Percy Spence has been commissioned to
illustrate it. The story, The Bulletin under-
stands, deals with the supposed fate of Leich-
hardt’s lost party and the mysterious cave-draw-
ings discovered by Grey. It will be a change to
have a boys’ book written and illustrated by two
men who really ought to know a little about
Australia.
The ’Murkan widow of the Duke of Marl-
borough (that literary duke, who used to paint his
first wife’s eye and elope with the spouses of his
brother peers) is credited with an intention to
marry Lord Bill Beresford, the Indian sport.
His lordship is a dashing hero of 47, and his wild
oats have been sown. The marriage would form
a family connection between Salmon Wilson and
the Marquis of Queensberry, and so give the
proud Victorian absentee a further interest in the
divorced nobility. Queensberry and the late
Marlborough (Lady Sarah Wilson’s relative) are
responsible for three divorced wives between
them.

Stoddarfc is a phenomenal Englishman,
being unassuming. Naturally, public applause on
the cricket-field is dear to him, and it adds zest to
the gratification he feels when he makes a century
against great bowlers. But off the field compli-
ments with him are out of place ; and, if he hates
anything, it is the extravagance of a toaster. When
Premier Reid subjected him to all the offensiveness
of direct personal flattery, Stoddart’s face was a
study. Poor Stoddart! which would he prefer—•
“a pair of spectacles ” or that one eye-glass ?
The recently-deceased Dr. Beaven Rake, of
Trinidad Leper Asylum, averred that leprosy was
associated with a very resistant bacillus, by which
the disease was communicated to others, but nob
to a great extent. He advocated the segregation
of lepers, and suggested that this might be facili-
tated by allowing or contriving marriages between
lepers, justifying his suggestion by the fact that
70 per cent of leprous couples are sterile. An
examination of leprosy-statistics further demon-
strates that less than six per cent of recorded cases
show inherited diseases, all other cases being those
of the offspring of perfectly healthy parents.
Of the late Lieut.-001. William Collard
Smith, ex-Minister for Mines (Vic.), who died
at Brunswick (Vic.) lately, it used to be told
that he once waited on David Syme, of Melbourne
Age, and said : “ I see that you always call me in
your paper, ‘ Mr. W. C. Smith ; ’ I wish that
you’d simply refer to me as ‘ Lieut.-Col. Smith.’ ”
“But aren’t your initials ‘W.C.S. ?’” “Yes,
but —er ” “Well, what?” “Don’t you see?
‘ W. C. Smith ’ —er —sounds—er, rather—er ”
Syme (blandly): “Well?” Smith (dramati-
cally) : “ Well ! ”
Sid. Gregory, nicknamed “ the Baby ” by
N.S.W. cricketers, scales 9st. 31b. and stands sft.
sin. The smallest man of the team, he stood up
best against the lightning deliveries of big
Richardson, and although that bowler puzzled
him much at times, he scored more freely off him
than off the other English bowlers. He has good
cause for punishing Richardson, for once when the
Australians played Surrey, a ball from the fast
bowler struck Gregory full on the forehead, flew
up, and was caught by a fieldsman 30 yards away.
Gregory was too stunned to hear the fieldsman’s
appeal for a catch. Speaking of the interest
taken in cricket, he says: “ English girls
understand the game better than Australian;
but English crowds take less interest than Aus-
tralian people, and in place of * barracking ’
they clap.” Gregory is possibly the best field in the
world. His returning is perfection ; he can throw
the ball 120 yards, which is an extraordinary per-
formance when compared with the giant Bonnor’s
best, viz., 124£yds. Briggs, the strongest thrower
on the English side, can only reach 110yds.
Gregory expects the Australians to have the best
of the test matches. “ In Australia we can beat
the English ; in England they can beat us.” Sid.
was born and bred in the atmosphere of cricket,
the name of Gregory having been deeply graven
on the scroll of Australian cricket fame by father
Ned and uncles Dave and Charley. Young Sid.
took to the family failing as a young duck takes
to water, and when scarcely out of his knicker-
bockers was playing in senior cricket. As a
deadly “throw-in,” Gregory runs the old-time
champion, Charley Bannerman, “to a head.” At
the beginning of the present season Gregory was
“ going off,” but he awoke to the gravity of the
situation, and after making big scores in club
cricket, reached the goal of most cricketers’
ambition by scoring a double century in an innings
in the first English test match.
Lieut. Chetwynd, the Ringarooma scape-
goat, seems to have had harsh treatment. The
evidence of Lieut. Hibberd says :
Lieut. Chetwynd was on duty almost continuously dur-
ing t he 29th and 30th August, and during the previous 10
days he had been on duty almost as continuously. . . .
At the commencement of the last cruise witness thought
it advisable to suggest to the captain that another officer
should be detailed for the watch Lieut. Chetwynd was
keeping.
Thus, Lieut. Chetwynd was practically dismissed
his ship because he could not keep awake after 12
days’ “ almost continuous ” duty.
The U.S. Army and Navy Journal has
the following, by an English engineer on the
Chinese cruiser Yang-Wai, re the treatment of
those disabled in an encounter with the Japanese:
There were but two white men aboard the cruiser—
myself and the surgeon. As soon as the vessel was out of
danger from the shells of the Japanese gunboats, he and
I, with a corps of the surgeon's assistants, set about
clearing up the terrible mess on the main deck. Wherever
we found a man hopelessly wounded, the surgeon gave him
instant relief from his agony. He cat ried an atomiser
filled with prussic acid, and as soon as his hasty examina-
tion of a hopeless case was finished, he sprayed the poison
into the man's mouth and nostrils. The dying men
craned their necks forward eagerly in their desire to
escape from the torture they suffered. There were very
few of the wounded who were not put out of their miseiy
by the surgeon’s atomiser. One of the most distressing
features of an engagement like this is the terrific noise
aboard a modern man-of-war fairly in action. The Y»ng-Wai
fought all the time with one screw turning slowly, her fires
raked clean, and the steam blowing off like the shrieking
of a gale. The scream of the shells, the thud and the
vibration as they struck the cruiser, the terrific roar as
they burst, and the deafening noise and concussion of our
own guns, made it utterly impossible for anything else
to be heard aboard the vessel. To my dying day I shall
remember that July 12th, and, most of all, the horrible
noise and the scene on the bloody main deck.
The world is getting more civilised. In Nelson’s
time they simply threw the wounded sailor over
the side alive, and the sharks got him. Now they
give him prussic acid with a squirt.
The retrenchment party in N.S. Wales
succeeded, after a number of exceptionally
long sittings, in “saving”—the sums “saved”
will probably all come up smiling again on the
supplementary estimates —a little over .£7OOO out
)f an estimated expenditure of about .£4,800,000.
Taking it all round, this will just about pay for the
printing and setting of the windy speeches about
nothing in particular by those brand-new leaders
of the retrenchment party, Richard Sleath and
Foghorn Miller. It is entirely due to these two
heroes that so little genuine retrenchment has been
effected. When the estimates first came on this
pair of statesmen rushed at them like bulls at a
gate, moving unreasonable reductions without any
discrimination whatsoever. This led to an under-
standing between the two old parties, with the
result that “sooner than hand the business over to
these fellows ” the Opposition voted solidly with
the Government.

To meet an insistent demand that this
characteristic Australian book should be procur-
able in more permanent form, “A Golden
Shanty,” by Bulletin writers, is now supplied in
neat cloth binding at 25., post free to any address.
It contains some of the best and most original
work which has helped to make The Bulletin
famous, and no Australian library is complete
without it.

POINTS.
Hia Holiness the N.S.W. Postmaster-
General.
Premier Turner promises that a Bill to
extend the franchise to women will be introduced
in the Victorian Parliament next year.
The present Queensland Government has
twice been saved from defeat by the Speaker’s
casting-vote, and once by a majority of one vote
only! „
It looks as if the author of those tremen-
dous Sunday paper tirades against a Major-
General was inspired by a very big politician
indeed.
Premier Turner, though successful, is half
worried to death, and is strongly disposed to
throw up the sponge. His law-partner predicted
as much.
Bryan O’Loghlen, Q. 0., is being com-
forted in his political exile with briefs from the
Vic. Crown. One would have thought that, after
the Matthew Davies nolle prosequi, the Crown had
lost confidence in Bryan as a legal adviser.
Now that there is a vacancy in Christ
Church (Sydney) incumbency, the N.S.W. Govern-
ment should take steps to finally settle the ques-
tion of the tenure under which the Church holds
its glebe lands. The question is one of huge prac-
tical importance to the State.
Minister Carruthers told a Sydney D.T.
reporter how grieved he felt that £lOOO had been
knocked off the vote for beautifying the Kuring-
gai Chase reserve; but, says the D.T. in effect,
the amount will be spent on it all the same.
“ Responsible ” Government!
Australian Star, after quoting some re-
marks made about Commandant Hutton by ex-
Premier Dibbs, says:—“lf the statements are
true ” &c. What an “if ”to come from the lead-
ing columns of the champion sycophant to the
ennobbled renegade Republican!
Jacob Garrard says there are no round
pegs in square holes in the N.S.W. Education
Department. This does not exactly disprove the
common allegation that there are many square
jiegs in round holes in that branch of the service ;
and that Jacob himself is the most rectangular
of the lot.
The S.A. Woman Suffrage Act—which
The Bulletin noticed when it passed the Council
some months ago—is now through the Assembly,
and only waits the formality of Victoria’s assent
to be law. The Might of Man will soon follow
the festive plesiosaurus and the cheerful ptero-
dactyl to the realm of Kingdom Gone.
Frank Madden, about the
most aggressive mediocrity in
theViclorian Assembly, wants
the country to “ ask time from
its creditors ’’ instead of levy-
ing new taxation. To ask for
time means that the country
would pay part of the interest
as it falls due and give its
creditors debentures which
would bear interest in their
turn—for the balance; in other
words, part of the interest
would be paid and the rest
added to the public debt. The
result is exactly the same as
if Victoria floated a new loan
and paid the interest out of it,
only the obtuse Madden can’t
see a simple thing like that.
Besides, unless more revenue
is raised it isn’t time that
Victoria wants, but eternity.
Another N.S.W. Civil
Service Commission is sitting,
and drawing big fees, and pro-
fessing to devise new means qt
economy. The other day it
examined Under • Secretaries
Critchett Walker and Kirk-
patrick, and it will examine
several dozen other officials
later on, and then draft a
stupendous report. This is the
old way of tackling the subject
of retrenchment. The new
way would be to appoint a
keen-witted and reliable man,
under some pretence or °tber,
to each department —he could
be sent to whitewash the ceil-
ings, or fresco the walls, or to
copy out half-a-ton of old docu-
ments, or anything and let
him bring in his report quite
unobtrusively at the end of
six months or so. He would
probably find out a whole lot
of things which even the best
Under - Secretary, sitting
placidly in his own apartment,
never sees or even dreams of.
A solicitor who is also a
justice of the peace has a big
start over the common or_ gar-
den attorney. Petty criminals
always engage him, he having
the power to hold them to bail,
and accept any bondsmen he
chooses; besides having freer
entree to the gaol. In the interests of fair play, no
solicitor should be a justice.
By the new S.A. Suffrage Act, adul
woman in that province can not only exercise a
vote, but may be elected to and sit in Parliamen .
Sexual weakness and idiosyncrasy had to oe
specially allowed for by clauses permitting women
resident more than three miles from a polling-
booth, and females who may be near that anxious
time for which their lords are partly responsible,
to take advantage of the Absent Voters’ Act and
record their votes under its machinery. Shades of
Joanna Southoott I
A correspondent:—The Brutus-like purity
of the Reid Government received new illustration
recently, when the brother of a prominent Free-
trade member was appointed to an inspectorship
of (say) tomatoes, for which his qualifications are,
to say the least, dubious. Roughly speaking, this
must make the 900th inspector of nothing-in-par-
ticular in N.S.W. civil service.
Out of every 30 adult males in South Aus-
tralia one is a Justice of the Peace. And out of
every 30 Justices of the Peace one is a Member of
Parliament. The only gentlemen in the Assem-
bly who do not possess the affix are Opposition-
leader Downer and Lands Minister Gillen. The
latter is a country storekeeper, and he doesn’t
wish to offend his customers by fining them 25..6d.
If the yarn told by Mike Duffy, M.P., a s
to his being snapped out of the late historical
division in the Q. Assembly by the dropping of
the bar by the Sergeant-at-Arms, is to be taken
seriously, then Premier Nelson owes his continu-
ance of office and fat salary to a pure fluke, and
occupies one of the most contemptible positions
that polities ever doled out to a supposedly free
leader.
The Queensland Govt,, in selecting repre-
sentatives for the Federal Council meeting, has
for the first time on record ignored the Opposi-
tion. Formerly, representatives were chosen
from both sides of the House. The explanation
on this occasion will probably be that there is no
Opposition tV select from. It is a point of Bris-
bane Fat Main etiquette not to be aware of
Glassey’s existence.
Bartlett, M.P., affirmed in the S.A.
Assembly, the other day, that every lawyer in the
House obtained a big fat fee to look after the
interests of the capitalists when the Pastoral Bill
was under consideration. “ When you don’t
understand a thing that the lawyers are fighting
hard for,” said Bartlett, “ be on the safe aide and
vote against it.’’ And Solomon, in all his glory,
never uttered a wiser precept.
Queensland brewers were registered under
the Beer Duty Act—repealed. The Liquor Act
enacts that no brewer but a registered brewer can
sell beer. Query : Since the Act under which all
Bananaland brewers were registered has been
repealed, are there any registered brewers at all
in Bananaland ? Therefore, can any Queensland
brewer legally sell beer? A teetotaller with a
turn for laying unexpected informations might try
this point.
Among the N.S.W. Loan Estimates for the
year are some curious items such as : railway trial
surveys, £9OOO (as the railways may never be
built, or may be built on some altogether new
route, this can’t be classed among reproductive
public works) ; repairs to mint machinery, &c.,
£5OOO ; thinning out forests, £50,000 ; patching
and altering an old dredge, £6500; and a few more
trifles of a similar character. The great rule of
N.S.W. finance is to charge nothing to revenue
wliich can, with any show of decency, be put into
the Loan Estimates, and though the above
examples are nothing like so bad as many things
done in the past, the Assembly should kick none
the less.

A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
It has long been known to scientists that for
improving the appearance and beautifying the
face oatmeal is far and away superior to all other
foods, but it had one drawback—the tendency to
heat the blood. To overcome this defect the at-
tention of eminent scientists has been devoted to
the removal of this imperfection ; and now, after
long years of careful and diligent research, art has
triumphed, and Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats is the
result, for, by skilful manipulation in the manu-
facture, all the heating properties have been ex-
tracted, so that now a beautiful complexion of
peach and ivory may be had by anyone who makes
a continuous use of Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats.
the vacillating; Sydney Smith, the simpering;

smasher to smithereens—the dancing dervish of
politics, ethical griminess and hypnotic exulta-
tion 1 •

A new Ass society has arisen in Brisbane
from the ashes of the insolvent old one. Theeminent
Bulcock is chief engineer; low-wages White is
second ; and not a man on the committee weighs
less than 25-stone of Fat. The gags are the same
ohl gags about “opposing class legislation ” — i.e.,
anything but property class legislation; and
securing representatives, not delegates, for Parlia-
ment—i.e., property representatives.
On 18th December the N.S.W. Assembly
referred the Jerilderie-Berrigan railway to the
Public Works Committee. And on the 19th the
Public Works Committee rushed together, and
took half-an-hour’s evidence about it, and charged
the usual fee for a sitting. It was the only job on
hand, and the committee apparently was m a
hurry to get at it, and yet wanted to make it last
as long as possible, and it succeeded admirably in
both respects.
Dear Bulletin, —lf Gov. Duff is worth
£lO,OOO a year, what is Mr. Eddy’s value? Some-
thing over £1,000,000, I think. If ever N.S.
Wales was a fool it is in allowing the Chief Com-
missioner to go for the sake of a few paltry
hundreds or thousands. He is the one man in
Australia at present in my opinion whose worth
can hardly be estimated. If I had my way, I’d
put him on pretty nearly every State department
to remodel it—and hang your Royal Commissions.
—Yours, Native.
Mr. D. Ham, M.L.0., says that there are
plenty of men outside willing to do the Vic. Civil-
service work for less than a third of the present
salaries. No doubt. There are also plenty of
men camping in the parks and gardens who would
be willing to do Ham’s legislative work for the
privilege of being allowed to go to sleep periodi-
cally upon the cushioned-seats of the Legislative
Council. Mr. Ham’s idea seems to be that Vic-
toria will never be truly great until its Govern-
ment becomes the boss sweater of the province.
Pratt, M.L.C., has followed the lead of his
local chieftain, Sargood, by resigning his position
in the Vic. Ministry. The gentleman’s position
was strictly honorary. Nobody had remembered
Pratt’s existence until he advertised it.
Whisper told the balmy wind,
“ Mr. Pratt has just resigned 1 ”
Which brought the question mocking—
“ Who the doooe is Mr. Pratt,
Will you kindly tell us that ?
And is the country rocking ? ”
John “Jannery” Davies, M.L.O. (N.S. W.)>
the Casual Labor Relief boardsman of yore ■
“Why should not boys have liberty as well
as men ? People should be allowed to work
as long as they like. If they chose to work
10 or 12 hours a day, they should be per-
mitted to do so.” And the N.S.W. Legis-
lative Council said —“Amen!” Of course, by
the Council’s logic, no one ever works 10 or 12
hours a day because he must do it or else get the
“sack;” he only does it because he wants to.
“Liberty,” in the bright lexicon of “Jannery”
Davies, always means liberty to work 12 hours a
day.
Patterson, big and burly, ruled his Parlia-
ment with bluster and brag, but achieved nothing.
Turner, dapper and blushing, has the appearance
of yielding all along the line, but the work gets
done all the same, and his bills get through. He
refuses to be led away from his point by inter-
ruptions, is placidly deaf to small insults, lets
personal explanations slide, pacifies indignant
cranks personally instead of leaving them to move
the adjournment of the House to air their
grievances, and keeps the Parliamentary machine
always oiled. A Premier who could part from
Sargood without telling that bore what a bump-
tious mockery he was is a marvel of good-
humored forbearance.

A monument of
colossal folly is the
Bundaberg - Glad-
stone (Q.) railway.
To please local au-
thorities, a bit was
built at each end. A
gap was then left
artistically in the
middle. No funds are
available to finish the
gap; the Gladstone
portion is rotting away ; railway-iron sufficient to
complete the line is also rotting away—altogether
a fine sample of political railway.
When N.S. W. metropolitan jury courts
rose for vacation last week, 33 suits were still un-
heard, and must now stand over for another term.
In one court no less than 21 cases were thus post-
poned. Nevertheless, several judges have been
“ holidaying ” off-and-on during the past term,
some even adjourning the court two hours before
time so as to get ready for a fete at Government
House, or some other social celebration. Thomas
Walker’s forlorn ambition to compile a time-table
for judicial functionaries presents an aspect, of
reasonableness when such facts are reviewed.
Premier Seddon (M.L.), who lately stated
that a K.C.M.G.-ship “ would come more grace-
fully at the close of a man’s political career,” has
begun to realise that his democratic constituents
have no good feeling towards a man to whom a
title comes at any time, whether gracefully or
Otherwise. His unlucky saying follows him like a’
shadow. At a recent function at Ahaura the
skeleton rose for the twentieth time, or more,
when a sour-looking citizen up-ended himself with
the old, old query: “Would the Hon. Mr.
Seddon accept a knighthood if it was offered
him?” Whereupon Premier Dick smote the
' 'vble with his mighty fist and answered “NO !!! ’’
N.S.W. Government has declaret. 1) that
it has no record of the Dibbs-Duff correspondence
respecting the Governor’s refusal to appoint cer-
tain nominees to the Legislative Council; (2) that
it has all the said correspondence, but that one
letter is officially private as between Duff and
Downing-street; (3) that it would be unfair to
Duff to publish the correspondence without this
“officially private” document; and (4) that
Downing-street’s leave to publish is being asked.
To which may be replied—(l) the N.S.W. Go-
vernment had the record all along; (2) the corres-
pondence has been printed by the Government
Printer ; (3j the printed copies have been shown
privately to political favorites.

An Australian Supreme Court Judge once
placed on record his roundaboutly-expressed
opinion that, under given circumstances, “any
man worthy of the name” would deliberately com-
mit perjury in order to save lovely but luckless
woman from the slow agonies of death on the
social antheap. Within the last few days, a man
who lied thus before a Divorce Court, in the
endeavor to save his sister from being officially
mantled in shame, was rewarded by another Judge
with a long term of penal servitude.
* • *
One day recently, when C.J. Prendergast
was sitting in the Supreme Court, Wellington,
with a long list of motions in banco before him,
he found most of the counsel not ready to go on.
The chief asked the cause of the unreadiness, and
inquired if any races were being held. “ I think
it is a tennis match, your Honor,” suggested the
junior counsel. “Tennis, tennis. Ah, when L
was a young man,” remarked the court, “we
found it necessary to work ; now they seem to do
nothing else but spend their afternoons chasing a
quinine pill around a cow-paddock.”
• • *
Chief Justice Madden, Vic., a bald and
astrachan-trimmed person, with the head .of- a
benevolent bruiser, and an elaborately spiked
moustache, has been advising the boys of S. Melb.
College to cultivate the Muses. “ Lovers,;’.’..said
his Honor, “ should learn the value of poetry in
raising their ideals, and then no one would ever
again put forward for serious discussion the ques-
tion, ‘ls marriage a failure ?’ ” And yet Shelley
and Byron, for instance, must have known some-
thing of the value of poetry in raising their ideals.
The fact appears to be exactly the opposite to
Judge Madden’s idea : the more poetry there is
infused into love’s young dream, the greater the
likelihood of a bad time after the prosaic awaken-
ing. No ordinary woman can ever be happy with
a poet-husband. The man who can provide Mary
Jane Smith with a Heaven on earth is the fat and
prosperous trader who drives her in his own buggy
to the races.
• • •
Coolgardie is gradually becoming civilised.
It is now only 66 per cent, less civilised than
Sydney. You can buy there for 9d. a “long
beer,” which costs 3d. in the East. True, it is
“shypoo” (as W.A. beer is called), but, after a
few months about Coolgardie, a man can swallow
anything liquid.

A certain Austra-
lian legislator has just
had an experience
wliich points an excep-

Mr. T. A. Westwood, the ex-Rue A us-
tralian, states that while he was lately on CooP
gardie, 29 deaths took place during one day-
Another Adelaide gold-seeker, recently returned,
says that during his stay on the field he met eight
funerals one day and nine the next.
The Coolgardie funeral is a primitive affair. In
a large number of cases no one distinctly remem-
bers the name of the deceased or where he came
from. Sometimes there is some friend^or^casu.
acquaintance to follow the remains to the ceme-
tery ; oftener there isn’t. The coffins are made
mostly of deal boards. The cemetery is a howling
wilderness of dust. The graves average 2ft. deep,
and every time a new one is opened a stench arises
from the mouldering relics of mortality next door.
Sometimes there is a burial-service ; at other times
there is nothing but a violent hustle to get the
hot, lugubrious function over. Then the pub.-
keeper lets the deceased’s bed to the next comer,
and he sleeps in the deceased’s room, among the
deceased’s bedclothes, all of which are just as the
last tenant left them. There is little ceremony at
Coolgar<lie, not much sentiment, and no sanita-
tion. Dust and death, old meat-tins, huge, black
flies, ghosts, disease, ill-luck and gloom are the
prevailing characteristics of the new land of gold*
• # *
Once more Sydney University authorities
are advertising a reward for the conviction of the
person who stole certain examination-papers. In
this case the authorities should have done justice
to the printers, and made it known that no sus-
picion rests upon them. Considering the tempta-
tion, it is simply marvellous that notone confiden-
tial piece of work in ten thousand entrusted to
printers finds its way to persons who might make
improper use of it. Fully three weeks before the
stoppage of certain Australian banks in the panic
period of ’93, documents, which would have given
the whole miserable game away, were entrusted
to metropolitan printers, and yet nothing leaked
out. This is the more remarkable as the Typo-
graphical Association of N.S.W. had funds in-
vested with one of the falling banks to the amount
of .£6OO. •
# • •
The pathetic tale of Rue Australia keeps
cropping up in all manner of places._ The latest
phase of the tragedy concerns the wives of t_wo of
the.settlers. • They were left behind at the original
exodus when their husbands went on to Paraguay,
arrangements being made for them to follow when
the Royal Tar took its next cargo. When the
Tar returned it was announced that no married
women would be allowed on that trip, so they
waited and possessed their souls in patience until
the.v'essek made its third voyage. But there never
was,any third voyage, and the whole business fell
through. ' Their passages were already paid, and
their worldly aU was in the hands of the Associa-
tion, and the Association was insolvent and
practically burst-up. The ■ “ camp ”- at Leich-/
hardt, where a crowd of 17 weary expectants were
huddled into three rooms waiting for the im-
possible to happen, was shifted to Balmain, and
the Balmain camp was presently shifted altogether
by the bailiffs for non-payment of rent.
After .that the two, forlorn grass-widows
earned a" precarious existence for themselves
and their children by badly-paid toil, and
spent their spare time in haunting the office of tha
Rue Australia Association and inquiring about
that ship' which never returned. They can’t get
to Paraguay, and their husbands in Paraguay
can’t get back to them. They are referred
vaguely from Head to Gilbert Casey, and from
Gilbert Casey back to Head, and find both these
and many other boss pioneers equally hopeless.
Their only consolation is to think of the 70 or so
families, also victims of “true mateship,” who
are in the.same tight place. Rue Australia seems
to have laid a Plight upon everyonewho touched
the ill-fated and ill-managed enterprise.

A Victorian money-lender has got a
drinky Britisher in durance. Tne latter receives
a regular remittance which, by reason of his in-
firmity, Mosenstein is authorised to collect.
Moseustein has also an authority to receive his
letters. He is kept at one of Mosenstein’s houses
in charge of a housekeeper of the elderly Flossie
description. There is a “To Let” notice up, and
if anyone calls he is informed that the sodden
Britisher is not there, that the place is vacant, and
the Flossie is merely a caretaker. Consequently,
his local acquaintances never find him. And as
he simply lives to drink, and never wants to go
out, no one else is likely to get hold of him.
These methods enable Mosenstem to annex three-
fourths of the income, and, by-and-bye, when the
seedy remittance man dies of the whisky-disease,
he will probably get all of it.

There is a new split in the Sydney Woman-
hood Suffrage League, on the same old rock
of offence, the “ marriage question. ’ ‘
Christian women,” they could not- stand
Mrs. President Wolstenholme’s “ vie ws ” on
this subject. And why should they ? The
political voting-power has really no more to do
with this matter than with knickerbockers, and
blue stockings. “Seek ye first the suffrage”
should be their word of order—and all these things
shall be added unto you—perhaps. By the way,
the hen-franchise would probably by now have
been an accomplished deed in N.S.W., if a few
years ago a certain -leading suffrage lady had
accepted an invitation to lunch with the wife of
a prominent —statesipan, that’s the word.

A Melb. suburb is named Pigopolis, for
Chicagoan reasons. The other Sunday, a new
preacher, unaware of the swinish genesis of the
locality, descended on the principal church. Its
foundations are laid in pork ; the pork people abso-
lutely own it. Some imp,made him select the
miracle of the herd of swine. Finding it caught
on, he enlarged with theories on the reason, dilat-
ing on the multiform diseases engendered by pork,
and the wisdom of the Jews.
Journalist : The W.C.T.U., Melb., with
its Mothers’-meeting for the lowly, recalls
similar meetings held awhile ago in Colling-
wood by very fashionable ladies. A gen-
teel comrade of mine used to report them
with enthusiasm, but I got the chance one
week, and pointed out that there were seven
ladies lecturing six women, in a crowded neighbor-
hood. My contention was that they were too
“stand-off,” and would never do any good on that
system. Whereupon the chief fair fugle-woman
wrote a bitter letter, “ and would have your re-
porter understand that, for a time being, we meet
these persons on a footing of perfect equality.”
To which my retort was that this Lady Bountiful
business, “fora time being,” was precisely what
the working women wouldn’t have at any price.

Sam uel Hordern, a sketch of whom
appear s in this issue, is the commodore
of the Prince Alfred Yacht Club —an
institution that has been established
some 27 years, and may be regarded as
the premier “live’’ yachting club in
Port Jackson. The Prince Alfred —a
name with a nasty anti-Australian taste
—numbers now some 80 odd members,
most of whom belong to the “ Royal ”
Yacht Squadron. “Sam,” as he is
familiarly called, has been identified
with yachting ever since he was able to
handle a tiller. He is now the owner of
two of the best steam yachts in Sydney
harbor. It was he who imported the
Cowes 2J-rater, Bronzewing, which easily
beat the Bulbul two or three times, and
which was purchased by “Mister” James
Fairfax, who, speaking generally, divides
with Sam Hordern the patronage of
local aquatic amateurs. It is pretty well
known that last year’s anniversary
regatta would have fallen through but
for Mr. Hordern, who has a special fancy
for the continued celebration of that
annual event. He has been for three
years president of the P.A.Y. Club,
and the present is his second term as
commodore. Local rowing also claims
Mr. Hordern as a patron. The Sydney
Rowing Club owes to him the Sam
Hordern boat. He is largely identi-
fied with the N.S. Wales turf, having
at Rand wick a training stable, wherein
are now between 30 and 40 horses, under
the care of Ike Barnshaw. Horse-racing
with Hordern is not a money-making
speculation, bub an outlet for super-
fluous energy and ample resources—some
of the prices paid by Sam for racing sires
border on the sensational—Nordenfeldt,
<£5000; Cannon, £2000; Realm (Sydney
Cup winner, ’92), <£2ooo ; being a few
examples. Mr. Hordern has placed a
100-guinea shield for competition in the
electoral cricket-matches this season, and
has given a 100-guinea trophy to be
shot for by the members of the Sydney
Gun Club. He is prominent amongst
N.S.W. breeders of stud-cattle, his
animals having carried off some of the
best prizes at the last metropolitan
agricultural show. A few weeks ago,
he gained the prize in pigeon-homing
from Melbourne to Sydney, and amongst
his recent triumphs in other exhibits
have been prizes fer canaries and for a
collie dog. Sam Hordern is the sole
member of the old firm of Anthony Hor-
dern and Sons, whose universal-provid-
ing establishment is its own best adver-
tisement.
One of Victoria’s imported military swag-
gerers, being a bachelor at the time of his appoint-
ment, had a clause put in his bond to the effect
that if he undertook matrimony in the beastly
kawliny his wife and family should be shipped
“hone ” at the b.k.’s expense. In due course he
found a moneyed wife, as per anticipation, and
also in due course the “.wife and family” passage-
money was applied for. Fortunately, the family
hadn’t got higher than number one when the
warrior’s term expired - Shortly after this a
brother Himperial officer became engaged to a
local heiress, whereupon he rushed up to the Vic.
war office with a demand for the spinster’s passage-
money. However, in this case there had not been
a matrimonial clause in the agreement, so the
seventy guineas didn't come off.

Fashionable society is often very mean
and not ashamed, or, at least, not until it is found
out. At a recent concert in the Vic.- Prahran
Town Hall for a very hard-up charity, a youth
who had the causa much at heart, the courage
of his convictions, aqd some experience, stood
at the door with his collection - plate; and
when different groups of people in the very
height of fashion said they had left their purses
behind them, he referred them to the note on the
programme which said that a small contribution
would ensure admission. Then they had to face
the alternative of going away or admitting they
had got their purses. They all chose the latter,
because it was a “ society ” affair, and they
would rather pay 3d. than be out of it.

And at Newcastle, Brisbane and Toowoomba, Queensland.
members, likewise, as a class, have larger familier
than the average. Church choirs are proverbially
“affectionate;” so are church members in general.
The most religious nations have a higher per-
centage of illegitima f e births than the least
religious. Most of the new and grotesquely
enthusiastic sects which spring up from time to
time have a certain amount of hugging in their
creed, and the members have a yearnful way of
calling each other “ brother ” and “ sister.” All
these, and many other facts of the same kind,
are evidences in support of the contention
that the religions instinct and the girl instinct
are mere varieties of the same feeling; in fact,
that they are the same instinct. The man
who has a yearning after pretty girls and tries not
to give way to it, becomes a parson and has a
large family. The man w' o hasn’t, becomes a
doctor and has no family, or else a very small one.
Materialists, and other unbelievers, as a rule,
have small families. No ordinarily-constituted
person can have the true yearnfulness after pretty
girls and yet be a materialist. These rules, by
the way, don’t apply much to Calvinists. Cal-
vinism, in its genuine form, is largely a scheme
for damning pretty girls, and the soulful variety
of person never belongs to that sect.

A Sydney pressman tells a strange storj
about a big Australian statesman whom he wai
once told off to interview. The statements of the
legislator were of such a reckless and incriminat-
ing character that the reporter’s hair fairly stood
on end. When he handed in his copy, the “sub”
promptly drew his pen through it, and said it
could not be true, as the lion. Blank would not,
under any circumstances, expose himself and
others in such a foolish fashion. The pressman
thereupon called on the politician, and told him
that the interview he had had with him on the
previous day would not be credited, and asked
if Ile would give a note certifying to the correct-
ness of the statements made. “Certainly,” was
the reply; “but first read your report of the
interview.” The literary man complied ; the
statesman heard him out, and then said, as he
opened the door, “ You have two minutes to get
clear of this house, and the next time you are sent
to interview anyone, don’t get drunk ; that’s all!”

» m •
The “prohibition-order” system is on the
verge of a break-down in Maoriland. According
to law, when a magistrate issues an order setting
forth that John Smith or John J. Jones is not to
be supplied with drink, every publican within a
certain radius has to paste the document con-
spicuously on the wall of his bar. The average
small publican now finds that he can’t comply
with the law much longer because all the walls of
bis bar are full of prohibition orders, and shortly
there will be no space left. Then something will
have to break. Even the most stringent drink
statute can’t make a bar any larger than it is.
• » •
In Australia, last year, 10 per cent, of the
buildings struck by lightning were churches.
Considering that churches don’t form one-quarter
per cent, of the buildings in the country, the
inference, according to the parsonical style of
reasoning, is that Providence is 40 times as angry
at churches as it is at public-houses and tote-
shops. Either that, or else the lightning is simply
allowed to strike the tallest building that comes
in its way, in which case the theory of an over-
ruling Providence must be altogether left out, and
then what becomes of the beautiful moral attached
to the story of the bad boy who was drowned
while fishing on Sunday?

Anglican children have had some desperately
comic “self-denial” suggestions offered them re-
cently. For instance, “To ask your clergyman
or Sunday-school teacher to get you a missionary
box, and often to try and get money put into it.’
The average boy invariably turns his little box
upside-down, so as to get money put out of it.
All that comes out is for the collector, and the
residue goes to the pore heathen.

The Melbourne Charity Organisation
Society is going bung. It has used up all
the funds in salaries and cab fares and sta-
tionery, and unless Fat Man comes forward
with more oil, this precious machine for grinding
the hopes of the indigent exceeding small will
have to stop working altogether. It has been a
• great boon to the “upper” classes for the last four
or five years. On the strength of his little annual
subscription to the C.O.S. old Buggins, M.L.0.,
was justified in referring 100 cases of immediate
distress per annum to the Society’s consideration.
The Good Samaritan just rubbed his hands and
sent the ragged petitioners along to the office in
Exhibition-st. where they were either denounced
as impostors or “put in the way of obtaining
help.”_ If they wandered from the right way, or
found it closed for repairs, and didn’t get any help
at all—well, the Society could help that.

Lunatic Cresswell, of the Parramatta
Asylum, is figuring again as Roger Tichborne.
The fresh “ evidence ” of Cresswell’s identity with
the missing man bristles with suggestions that his
discoverer, fi JVlrs. »Jenkins, is not well read in the
history of the Tichborne trial. By proving a long
intimacy between the crank and Arthur Orton,
who is said to have held possession of his partner’s
“ papers,” the lady merely proves the ab-
surdity of her case. Orton, when he first
put in his claim to the baronetcy, didn t
know the Christian names of old Lady Tich-
borne or anything else which he might have
learned from the “ papers ” aforesaid, and from
Roger himself. Moreover, he had no papers to
s how—and what has become of those valuable
documents, anyhow? The most crushing argu-
ment against the Rarrainatta Roger Ortons
present impecuniosity. If that fallen impostor
had ever been intimate with the man he imper-
sonated, and knew that he was living now, or
might be living, in Australia as Cresswell, he
would have turned the knowledge to account and
come over here to find his old chum long ago.
Orton, when last heard of, was a public-house
potman in very reduced circumstances. /
THE GAMBLER'S TWINS
5 -Ht, (111% ’
The house was hushed. An awful sense
Of something happening.
With mute, mysterious, straining tense.
Tugged hard at everything.
The slippered servants trode the stairs
As spooks in ether float;
Died even then the germs of prayers
In Utterance’s throat.
Yet muffled footfalls seemed to plunge
Along an unseen slope ;
A ghostlike maiden bore a sponge,
Another carried soap.
A worried gambler, reft of rest,
Betrayed no sign insane
Beyond a nose with vigor pressed
Against a window-pane.
A “ Wah !” A “Way !” He turned his head,
Each eye shone like a pebble—
“There were two cries,” he softly said,
“ Contralto and a treble !”
He whistled, “ Whew !” “Great Kokum Epps !
I wonder what’s the trouble?”
He turned his head, he turned his steps,
A halting human bubble.
A man of portly, grave repose
The chamber entered in ;
A man with specs, upon his nose
And folds upon his chin.
His manner was a gracious wheeze,
That added to his sins ;
“They’re all progressing well, and she’s
Presented you with twins !”
A pallor blanched the father’s face,
Fright set his tongue-strings loose :
“ I reckoned that she’d play the Ace,
She’s gone and led the Deuce !”

J.G.F. ; The Bulletin declines to advertise
your “leprosy cure” until it has been proved. _ Why don t

thing to that effect): You shed no new light on the China-

A painstaking inspection of “Cinderella,”
at Sydney Lyceum, suggests that the days of
pantomime are over. “ Cinderella ”is more like
a big variety-show than anything else, and it isn’t
even that in a very definite sense. There is a
ballet in it, which can be cordially recommended
as containing as little filmy drapery and as much
inspiriting legs as can well be inserted in the
space ; and a chorus of ladies in bathing-costumes
also deserves warm approval. The “festival of
sport ” is a new idea which appeals to the football
and cricket section of the audience; there is a
graceful procession of bicyclists ; a remarkably
good roller-skate performer, who is by no means
new; an acrobatic person, who is reasonably good,
but not new either; and sundry baby acrobats
who are new, but dismally commonplace. Each
of the latter bows profoundly and takes an
imaginary hair out of his mouth after each feat—
that same old hair which every contortionist
seems to be troubled with in the region of his
digestion. The show also contains two men in
women’s clothes, a baron with a weird story to tell
about his friend Macintyre, and the usual demons,
witches, fairies, and the rest. Mr. Horace
Wheatley, the old man who tells the tale about
Macintyre, is reported to have been specially
engaged in England for the part, and the most
caretul audit of Horace can show no just reason
for his importation. Something more or less to
the same effect may be said about Mr. James
Non ie, who makes “ his first appearance in Aus-
tralia ” as the prince, and reveals himself so far as
a gentleman with nothing in particular in the way
of a singing voice, and no very defined charac-
teristics of any kind.
# * •
The Bulletin had some idea of making
sundry enthusiastic statements concerning Miss
Ray J ones, the “ Cinderella ” of the show, but it
has decided to hang them over till a future oppor-
tunity. Miss Jones is a rather nice little lady,
who sings rather nicely, but she doesn’t lend her-
self to boundless enthusiasm. The security isn’t
good enough. The broad merriment of Martyn
Hagan, as the piincipal man in woman’s clothes,
and Horace Wheatley’s stories about Macintyre,
are the main supports of the pantomime
proper. The Bulletin’s friend Hagan is a
humorist of no mean order, and he sup-
plies nearly all the fun which there is in
“Cinderella.” An altogether commendable trans-
formation-scene helps to redeem the performance,
but, taking itall round, the Lyceum pantomime of
’94 is a rather heavy affair, and its indefinable
aspect of old age is vaguely suggestive of a baby
Chinaman. Saturday night’s audience showed a
painful inclination to take it seriously, and
nothing kills a pantomime faster than being taken
seriously. Also, as Time, in the respected person
of Mr. Hasker, crawled along on two platinum
feet, it developed an inclination to take the show
more and more seriously, and to be taken more
seriously is enough to kill any panto.
This is the rhyme of a pantomime that
was born before due date, like the father’s
boot which lovers compute for ten —and meet at
eight. And on Saturday night Her Majesty’s,
bright with girls in gay frocks and bunions,
seemed somehow to miss the aroma of geese with
a stuffing of sage and onions. Yet all went well
as the marriage belle who has planted six husbands
before, and who walks to the altar without a falter
and audibly asks for more. For indeed the show
has vim and go (because it is meant to stay ?) while
spots are few and three or two of the scenes are
distangay. True, the jokes are weak and nothing
to speak of lurks in the musical setting ; while no
bright star sheds glory afar or takes a strong lead in
the betting. But the company’s even, without any
leaven of norrid, incongruous sticks; and spec-
tacles splendid, until the play’s ended, quench
every desire to heave bricks.
*• ■ •
And in pantos, to see a more charming
idea The Bulletin never hopes, than Jack’s house
a-building by scores of elf-children with scaffolding,
bricks and ropes. So quaint and delightful, so
jolly and spriteful, so artfully guarded from
failure, is the mannikin co., that a better tableau
of its kind is unknown to Australia. Exceed-
ingly good, too, the scene in the wood,
where the witches prove really plump
fairies, in beautiful tights which are full of
delights for the veterans full of vagaries.
The lilliput dance, when the tiny tots prance
through the solemnest, sweetest of measures, is
another joy new, and the two mites in blue are
among the night’s darlingest treasures. The grand
transformation received an ovation, though some
in the. past have excelled it; while the old English
set took one’s fancy, you bet, and the card palace
easily held it.
• • •
The best solo was “ ’E dunno,” by a tenor
of half-a-score; but Florrie Forde was a boon
from the Lord when she sang of things done no
more. And then Fred Dark made a visible mark
as he walked out “on his own while the Ber-
mans’ fun was never done till “loyalty” got its
bone. Florrie was Jack, and always came back,
in an architectural spell, to kiss Florence Esdaile,
who made many a breast ail as the Princess Daisy
Bell. And, as for the rest, they gave of their best,
buc space unluckily fails ; so the reader must go
and see the show and fill in his own details.
Bland Holt’s new drama at Sydney Royal,
and Charles Arnold’s comedy season at the
Criterion, were both advertised for Wednesday
night, too late for notice in this issue. The vast
mass of spontaneous enthusiasm which The Bul-
letin had prepared for these two occasions is,
therefore, held over till next week. Mr. Arnold’s
season opens with “ Charley’s Aunt ” from Brazil,
where the dethroned emperors and the revolu-
tions come from, and the appearance of Mr.
Seagar in Thornton’s old part of the aunt is a
matter of interest. The Kinetoseope, in its old
premises in Pitt-street, is still in good health.
The Jubilee Singers burst forth into song at the
Centenary Hall on Xmas night; and the Flying
Machine proposes to make its more or less futile
endeavour on Boxing Day. Other shows are in
reasonably good condition, and exhibit none of
the disastrous symptoms mentioned in the patent
medicine advts.
** * *
The success of Hosking’s spirits and de-
materialisations —alas ! that Mrs. Besant should
have left without seeing those visions fade-at
Melb. Princess’ last week was slightly damped by
a preliminary lecturer. Nobody appeared to
hanker after any opening remarks, but the inter-
loper in question, whose name was left out of the
bill, held his ground and scoffed at the follies of
spiritualism until the amphitheatre wished him
dead, and signified the same in the usual manner.
He was a dauntless nuisance. As a soldier he
would probably earn the Victoria Cross in his first
engagement with the enemy. “The tricks of
mediums, so-called, cannot deceive us,” said the
lecturer, gazing earnestly at his weary audience,
“we know that our dear ones departed are with
God and of God.” Whereupon a voice from aloft
cried, “ O-o-o-h, God!” And still the gallant
hero stuck to his discourse.

Also, this journal vises, with pudding on
its chest and vine-leaves in its hair (“hair ’ is
a figure of speech) to repeat previous announce-
ments that Maggie Moure’s panto, at Melb.
Royal is called “Aladdin.” The betting is never
more than 5 to 1 against any pantomime being
called “Aladdin”—but no matter. George Dar-
rell, at the Opera House, is doubtless playing
“The Double Event” according to promise,
whilst Elton and a likely company are engaged
in a furious production of “The Judge” at the
Bijou. All these great events were due on Boxing
Night.

Dan Barry has burst forth again in Mel-
bourne—that is to say, Daniel was expected to do
it about the time of going to press.- lie produces
himself and company at the Oxford, by arrange-
ment with Coghill Bros., in “ Round the Clock.”
The Cogbills have fixed their headquarters at the
Cambridge Theatre, but Governor Hopetoun is on
tour.
** * *
Mr. George Darrell has taken a lease of
the Opera House, Melbourne, and will open on
Boxing Night in the racing drama, “The Double
Event,” with the beautiful blue ballet, the great
Melbourne Cup-scene, and entirely new scenery
by Grist and Little, A. L. Canard, acting-
manager.

Prof. Marshall Hall’s Melb. admirers are not satisfied
with his talent for making himself absurd. They insist
upon helpinsr him to be ridiculous. At his last concert
an infatuated maiden handed up to him a laurel-wreath
tied round with yellow ribbon. He looked as unhappy
with it as a bachelor with a baby.

The proper name of Harry Roberts, Maggie Moore’s
leading man, is Robertson, and he is a son of Mr. and
Mrs. Robertson, well-known in early Melb. theatrical
circles. He is a cousin of Forbes Robertson, the popular
London actor. Laura Roberts is Harry’s sister, by the
way.

Wanted—a complete list of the persons, male and
