these people in London, and they will speak unkindly to

Re spooks. How about these Japanese ones: “In
one of the booths a man stretched on a table, flat
on his back, is alone on the stage ; puppets of almost
human size, with horribly grinning masks, spring out of
his body; they speak, gesticulate, then fall back like
empty rags; with a sudden spring they start up again,
change their costumes, change their faces, tearing about
in one continual frenzy. Suddenly three, even four,
appear at the same time ; they are nothing more than the
four limbs of the outstretched man whose legs and arms,
raised on high, are each one dressed up and capped with
a wig under which peers a mask ; between these phan-
toms tremendous fighting and battling take place, and
many a sword-thrust is exchanged. The most perfect of
all is a certain puppet representing an old hag ; every
lime she appears with her weird head and ghastly grin,
the lights burn low, the music of the accompanying
orchestra moans forth a sinister strain given by the
flutes mingled with a rattling tremolo: which sounds like
the clatter of bones.”—Pierrf. Loti, from “ Madame
Chrymnthbme.”
Madame Heller, while doing the clairvoyant busmessat

would fall next session. About ’B2, by the way, an equally
celebrated clairvoyant announced at Brisbane that
Griffith would die within two years. But Samuel still
lives and moves and has £3500 a year, and the prophecy
is forgotten.

oa .’t see anything but splash. There is much good work

Su-'day opinion.
The American stage is in a bad way. Thousands are
“resting.” It is computed that the average salary of
the 5000 in work is about 35d01. (£7) a week for a six
months’ season, or £lBO as the average year’s income. As
the stars get whopping salaries the rank-and-file are very
poorly paid.

SPORTING
The remarks which an Australian ground crowd j
make are scarcely ever heard in pavilion or members
stand, but the players hear them only too well. When-
ever Gillen started to bowl at Svdney, a volley came from
the fence, “ Keep yourself on, George,” and so on.
Giffen kept himself on, and on, and on, but the
English say his bowling was the best, although Turner
bowled far better than his analysis shows.
* * ♦ ♦
Lockwood takes such an awkwardly long run
that the least miss or slip in his stride will no-ball him.
Thrice in two overs during the test-match Bannerman
called no-ball. In Adelaide, Lockwood bowled 10 no-
balls.
♦ ♦ * *
Jones, the Adelaide fast bowler, cannot reach
the pace of Lockwood or Richardson. He is a good foot-
baller with a footballer’s record ; he has had his nose
broken, his shoulder put out, both knees twisted, one
ankle broken, and the other badly injured. Last season
he was laid up with an injured ankle for seven weeks, and
lost his billet in consequence.
* * 1 ♦ ♦
Darling, the South Australian, is considered by
some of the Englishmen to be a better left-hand batsman
than ever Moses was.
♦ * * •
The full strength of English bowling is in this
team, and, not to boast, the Englishmen wanted it.
Richardson is considered by English critics, and by most
of the present team, to be the best English bowler living.
Lockwood has done feats to make a batsman’s hair up-
end; witness his 5 wickets for 7 in one county-match,
Surrey v. Nottingham; his 11 wickets for 40, all bowled,
Surrey v. Kent. On a sticky wicket he w ill bowl his fastest
and make nearly every ball break back a foot. His first
app' arance against an Australian Eleven was with
Nottingham in ’B6 ; his first ball bowled Bonnor. “She
was a fast one; she squatted on the pitch and shot in
low.” So Lockwood describes it. Peel is proverbial for
consistency, and Briggs is Grace’s pet bowler.
’*♦ » *
The English, in the test match, had bad luck
with their bowlers. The first Australian innings saw the
collapse of Lockwood ; he showed typhoidal symptoms for
two days, but by Monday was well again. Next, Richard-
son, after bowling Lyons at the 'start of the second
innings, gave up, his brain spinning ; he came back to the
dressing-room with a temperature of 102.
* ♦ ♦ •
To be bowled off the pads each innings of the
test-match was a tame death for the great Lyons. He
went in first, as usual, with Trott, in the second innings ;
he never felt better; he hit with might and main; he
broke a bat; he got another; he made 25 while Trott
made 1; then the pads which were meant to defend him
betrayed him to the enemy. Phillips, the English
umpire, smiled as the giant came back from the wjeket.
Was he thinking of the day when he met Lyons in thj
match Australia v. Middlesex, at Kennington Oval, and
sent the great smiter a tempting one to lure him to
destruction ? Lyons accepted the temptation, and
nearly killed a cab-horse by driving the ball clean out of
the ground, over the pavilion.
After five days’ play, and after 1400 runs had
been scored upon it, the Sydney wicket was easy and
true ; its durability was a revelation to those of the Eng-
lish who had never visited Australia before. “ Seeing is
believing,” said Lockwood. “I’d have thought it im-
possible for a wicket to stand like this; fine weather
makes fine wickets, and fine wickets make fine batsmen ;
we bat better here than we do in England.”
♦ * » »
Ford looked safe for a century when he made a
false stroke and was caught out for 48. He is highly-
strung and sanguine ; he returned to the dressing-room
with the keenest air of disappointment. He comes of a
numerous family—has ten brothers.
* * * *
The ball travels over the English fielding-ground
much faster than it does over the Australian. When the
Englishmen played Adelaide, the grass in the out-field
was 2in. long and greatly retarded the ball.
« * * *
Stoddart anxiously watched the close of the
second English innings in the Sydney test-match, clap-
ping his men vigorously at every good stroke, and
expressing his gratification because “we have made a
fight of it.” But his face beamed on Thursday morning,
after the rain had “ made a night of it.”
♦ * * *
Sid. Gregory compiled his 201 runs in 25 minutes
less time than it took Geo. Giffen to make 161.
* » ♦ ♦
Ward’s 75 and 117 in the test-match proved him
to be the most reliable bat in the English team, and hia
innings on each occasion was not marred by a single life.

At least two first-flight cornstalk cricketers are
making a gallant struggle to upset the hitherto unsur-
passable long-beer performances of a lately-retired, once-
briiiiant batsman.
* ♦ ♦ »
Junior cricketers bitterly complain of the 2s. fid.
per man for both elevens charged by Sydney Cricket
Ground trustees for match-wickets. The Domain charge
is only 9d. per man of one team, and 3d. of this is really a
special bonus to the caretaker.
* * ♦ *
Humphreys, the English lob bowler, has played
county cricket the last 23 years, and as the last English
season was exceptionally dry, he then excelled his previous
county records by capturing 122 wickets. Although he
likes fast wickets, the Adelaide wicket did not suit his
bowling; its peculiar “ glassiness ” takes all the devil out
of his ball. In his quarter of a century’s cricket, he played
for the first time on matting, when his deceitful lobs,
breaking well on the dark-blue pitch, bamboozled the
batsmen of Armidale: in that match he came out with
the fine analysis of 9 wickets at 5 runs per wicket. On a
sticky wicket—that is, a Turner wicket—he can break a
good-length, good-pace ball one yard. This visit, his first
to Australia, will probably close his long career as a
cricketer.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, — After reading much cricket-
literature, and listening to many' animatrd discussions
outside newspaper offices, I have discovered that Stod-
dart’s team can’t play with the worst of the luck, any-
how'. The Australians win the toss, and Lockwood, an
important item in the game, is prevented by a strain from
helping to get them out. The Australians made a big
score, which is solely accounted for by “magnificent bat-
ting.” Then the English go in and a lot of rain falls
during their innings, yet there is no bad luck about that.
The wicket “favors the bowlers a little,” but not much.
The proof lies in the fact that Stoddart and Co. are not
wiped out immediately—there is i o suggestion of fine bat-
ting in their case. Then the Englishmen have the good luck
to follow on (being 260 runs behind) upon a wicket which
“ rolls out almost as good as at the start,” when, by the
way, the other side had the use of it. And Blackham
hurts his hand—a splendid piece of luck for the English-
men ! The loss of the toss, the loss of a valuable bowler,
the spoiled wicket for their first innings—all these matters
were as nothing compared with the other side’s cruel
luck in losing Blackham. And, finally, the Australians’
horrible ill-fortune reached its climax in the second
innings, when 8 wickets fell for 53. The bowling and
fielding, of course, had a little to do with it ; but it was
the rain that did the real mischief. If three of the
Englishmen were to die during the match, the circum-
stance, “although decidedly unfortunate for the visitors,”
would “ in no wise detract from the merit of the Austra-
lian victory”—which, as it happens, is a defeat thanks to
“the visitation of God."’Yours, Britisher.
« * ♦ ♦
Comparisons between the match of last week
and the game played at the Oval in 1882 are quite absurd.
The seven runs by which the Australians beat England
represented about 2£ per cent, of the total number of
runs scored by both sides in one day. Stoddart’s team
won by § per cent, of the grand total scored in five days
and a bit. Enthusiasts may point to the figures
on the board, as Marc Antony pointed to the dead
figure of J. Caesar, and cry, “ Here was a cricket-match 1
When comes such another ? ”
When telegrams were posted in Melbourne on
the Monday, announcing “ Heavy rain ” and a temporary
suspension of play in the grt at match, several rash book-
makers set about “making a hit” by laying hundreds or
fifties to one on the Australians. The writer knows of
one young Hebrew who took £2OO to £3 10s. from another
Chosen Person.
Had the Englishmen gone in first and the posi-
tions of the teams been reversed—imagine the headlines
in the newspapers! “An Unparalleled Yietoiy. A Great
Game Gloriously Won. Wonderful Bowling by (say)
Giffen and Turner. Advance Australia!” As it turned
out, the headlines were modest in size, and most
ingeniously worded. The two Melbourne dailies an-
nounced respectively—“ Unexpected Defeat of the Aus-
tralians,” and “Victory snatched from the Australians.”
The English bowling got ne’er a mention at the top of
the column. After the score had reached 158 (with five
wickets to fall) Peel put up a record of 4 wickets, and two
easy chances missed off him, for 5 runs.
♦ * * *
“Johnny” Briggs, the best of the English
fields, brought his father 100 miles to see Gregory field
for the Australians in England. Briggs pater watched
Gregory most intently, and his verdict was, “I don't
think him better than you, Johnny 1” “ But he can throw
in better, father?” “ Well, it’s doubtful, Johnny.”
♦ * * *
Smokers and drinkers poll well in the teams
of the last big test cricket-match. Of the Englishmen,
amateur Ford is the sole non-smoker and teetotaller, but
Humphreys smokes rarely. All the Australians smoke,
and their one teetotaller is Darling, of Adelaide.
* * * *
Graham fielded splendidly in the test-match,
until Briggs hit a ball so fair into his hands that he had
not to budge an inch for it; he dropped it, and was almost
too crestfallen to look up again. Brown, the best outfield
on the English side, made the mistake of a novice in the
Adelaide match : misjudging the flight of a ball, he let it
drop a yard in front of him.

match by Trott for 62.

they also said, “Wait, cricket is cricket.’’ They made
325 in their first innings, leaving them 261 in arrears ;
nevertheless, they were all fight. M’Laren took a wager
of £2OO to £l4 against them, and Stoddart bet a level £2O
that his men would conquer. They were going to score
600 in their second innings. They reached 437 ; Gay being
the only one not able to obtain double figures.
The Australians only wanted 176 to win ;
Gregory had scored 201 himself in the first innings,
breaking the hearts of the English bowlers and earning
nearly £5OO in money laid him in hundreds and fifties to
nothing, for one Australian bookmaker stood to lose, and
did lose, many thousands by an English victory. As
the English lost Lockwood through illness for the first
Australian innings, so now they lost Richardson for the
second, and Richardson was the only bowler who had suc-
ceeded in puzzling Gregory, Giffen and Iredale. At 4
o’clock on the Wednesday, as a threatening bank of clouds
rose up in the south-east, Lyons came out with Trott,
and scoring 25 in 14 minutes, while Trott scored one,
showed how the match was to be won. But Lyons got
out then, and Giffen and Trott played maiden after maiden
to the English bowlers, who were cheered by the near
prospect of a downpour. There was that in the air which
said: “The cricketer proposes, but the weather dis-
poses.”
The Australian team, watching in the stand and
feeling the weather-influence, chafed at the tardiness of
the game. Gregory and Iredale said: “If we don’t get
130 to-night we shall be beaten on the wet wicket to-
morrow.” If Giffen had sent in Gregory and Iredale
instead of Trott and himself, the match, already virtually
won, would probably have been ended that evening. But
instead of the needed 130 the Australians scored 113 by
the day’s close, and on the morrow Peel and Briggs,
playing havoc on the sodden wicket, wrenched victory
from the very grasp of the Australian team, and the
English, who deserved to win, triumphed by 10 runs.

Stoddart denies absolutely the statements pub-
lished that he, at Armidale, N.S.W., thought that no

* « * »
Stoddart is most emphatic re interviewing : “ I
would have no pleasure in the tour at all if I were to be
interviewed. Any reports that have been published as
being interviews with me are absolutely untrue, lor I
have never given an opinion to a newspaper man on the
tour, at any of the towns I have visited. I hope you will,
therefore, excuse me in declining to say anything about
the match.” Now, the Adelaide papers printed “inter-
views ” and wired them to the other provinces.

The mammoth score of 536, first innings of the
Australians v. Stoddart’s, is the record for a test-match,
*he previous record being 551 made by Murdoch’s 1884
team at the Oval, Surrey. In this match three men
reached the century, viz., Murdoch 211, Scott 102, and P.
M ‘Donnell 103.
» * ♦ »
Some unspeakable ass cables from England to
lay that: “According to the Loudon Daily Chronicle, in
the recent test cricket-match the batting and bowling of
the Englishmen were, probably, superior to the Aus-
tralians, but in fielding the visitors were not equal to the
colonials. The London Daily News and Standard con-
sider that the first test cricket-match in Sydney was with-
out parallel in the history of similar contests.” The Aus-
tralian, apparently, hasn’t a definite opinion about an event
which he saw with his own eyes, until some unknown Eng-
lishman, who didn’t see it, supplies him with one from
16,000 miles away.
* * * •
The whole-souled Australian view of the test-
match is that “ our boys ” collapsed through wet rot.
The opposition view is that the awful fall at least partially
resulted from dry rot.

Four of the Australian team are Post-office
employes: Giffen aid Raedman in Adelaide; Trott in
Melbourne ; Gregory in Sydney.
* * * *
Trumper, the boy who made 67 in slashing style
for the 18 N.S.W. Juniors against the Englishmen, was 17
on 2nd Nov. last, and is a pupil-teacher at Crown-street
(Sydney) Public-school. It is a pity, by the way, that 18
Juniors were played against St.oddart’s team. The Eng-
lishmen mighthave routed a juniorllwith great slaughter,
but, at any rate, they would have been more on their
mettle against an 11. In an 11 versus 18 match neither
side can possibly get any glory. After their first day’s
batting the Juniors should have declared their innings
closed, and (if possible) fielded only 11 men against the
visitors.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, —My ascetic soul deviseth every
week new tortures for my sins. The Austral cycle became
the wheel of Ixion for me last week, “ high in the dome,
suspended, of hell,” as Browning hath it. This week a
regatta on the Yarra furnished forth my banquet of
torment. Half-naked men, shocking examples of city
physique, flitted hither and thither in crab-traps on the
river's brown bosom. Every now and again at the pop of
a pistol various nakednesses in crab-traps would shrill out
unmusical cheers, and go their way. Then I and the
othej wretches on the hanks would wonder if something
might happen soon. But nathing happened, except that
the evening paper spread an account of it over a weary
column. —Yours, respectfully, Y.Z.
The New Year’s Day meeting of the Y.R.O.
ought to show a profit this time. The stakes have been
lavishly decreased without affecting the number of
probable starters, and a January Ist attendance at Flem-
ington is always fairly satisfactory. But the Standish
and Bagot “ double ” has lost all its old interest for
punters now that there are not more than three certain
“ goers ” for any little handicap until the horses leave
the paddock. The Bagot used to be a £lOOO event.
• * * *
The Vic. Govt, is being urged to take proceed-
ings against the alleged Hollis-gun forgers. A queer
thing is the law, as administered. If you forge a cheque
for £1 a prosecution will automatically follow and you
will get three years, if not seven ; if you forge the maker’s
name on a gun you won’t be prosecuted unless somebody
forcibly reminds the Government that the deed is not one
to be quite squared by civil process.
* * * *
Walter Griffiths, M.P., the baby of the S.A.
Assembly, made 109 runs, not out, in the cricket-match,
Parliament v. Press, early in December. He just equalled
the scribblers’ entire score off his own bat.

Vicissitudes of the turf: A one-time owner of
one of the greatest horses that ever won a Vic. Derby
was up on a so-called “fraud’’-summons, the other day, for
a £3 debt, at a suburban court. Yet the same decent fellow
could have given the game up with £20,000 had he been
sensible. He intended to do so. He ventured one more
shot, and backed his horses to win a double of about
thirty “ thou.,” and lost. Tried again, and lost. Ditto 1
Ditto!! Ditto! 11

Middleton, the Dunolly miner, who won this
year’s Austral Wheel Race, is slobbered over in print as a
“ magnificent specimen of young Australia.” As a matter
of fact, whatever Middleton may be as a cyclist, he is
certainly no Apollo: tall, thin, and cadaverous his looks
certainly belie the list. 81b. he claims to weigh.
» ♦ * ♦
Thurgood, the Vio. champion goal-kicker, ap-
peared at the A. A. sports in Melb. last week, and won
putting the weight (161 b.) without an effort. Thurgood is
so splendidly-built that he should be about the first man
selected by Williamson and Musgrove for their male
living pictures.
The M. 8.0. takes something over J6lOOO out of
its two days’ Austral meeting. Surely it would pay
some enterprising individual to promote a similar one on
Sydney Association Ground. The stake of £2oo—which
looks so enormous to the naked eye—is nearly met by the
entrance and acceptance-fees of the competitors. This
year 94 cyclists each paid £2 fees.
* * ♦ ♦
Punters are at last afforded a chance of getting
at the cause of a certain Maoriland horse’s “in-and-out”
running. He is awarded fourteen stone in a certain event,
to allow, no doubt, of his carrying the stable jockey as
well as a representative of the punting fraternity, who
will offer suggestions to the jock during the race, and see
that the jock rides him properly. A similar system
applied to a few inconsistent bookmakers' horses would
be most gratifying to backers generally.
* » » •
Jenkins, the crack cyclist, will go for the five-
mile championship of the Crusaders Club at North Shore,
Sydney, on Deo. 15. and that will be his last race. Like
Hanlan, he thinks the athletes’ reign is only for 10 years.
He has been in training 18 years, and has raced against
men and time, all distances from J mile to 100 miles. He
has 79 prizes, of which first-prizes number no less than 57.
In 'B3 he put up the N.S. W. 100-mile record and in ’BB he
eclipsed himself by winning 17 first-prizes.
• * » *
Iredale has been a N.S.W. cricket representa-
tive since ’B7, when he scored 53 and 0 against Victoria.
Bad luck has befallen him as it does all other men and
cricketers ; one interprovincial season his average fell to
7 runs an innings, but still worse luck awaited him, for,
after earning a place by splendid batting in the ’92 Aus-
tralian eleven, his merits were passed over by the selec-
tion committee. Against English elevens and the teams
of Melbourne and Adelaide he has, during the past seven
seasons, scored over 1000 runs—average, 33 per innings.

The use of cocoanut matting for cricketing
pitches is a purely Australian idea, and came as a revela-
tion to the English Eleven of ’BB. O’Brien held that its
use would add two months to the English cricket season,
and prophesied that by ’B9 every club in the United
Kingdom would be using it. Has this prediction come offy
The success of the new Cannalin remedies
for piles is most remarkable, large quantities
being sent to all parts of the colonies from the
depot at 257 George-street, Sydney.
Cabled that Randolph Churchill lies para-
lysed at Marseilles.
Russell Sage, the New York financier,
boasts that he can lay his hands quickly on more
money than any other man in the world. He
keeps four millions in gold locked up in the strong
room of his bank.
A favorite dish with guests at Baron
Hirsoh’s castle in Germany is wolf and lobster
sauce. A slice of wolf undercut is reckoned very
choice, and no man can be said to have lived until
he has dipped his spoon into wolf-tail soup.
Mr. Lloyd Burdekin, medical student,
eldest son of Mr. Sydney Burdekin, the Sydney
millionaire, has, it is cabled, shot himself in Lon-
don, after writing letters announcing his intention
to commit suicide. So far, the causes of this dis-
tressing event are not clear.
The S.A. Govt, is liable for a further sum
of <£1,484,330 payable to squatters for improve-
ments on their leases. The intent of the present
Pastoral Bill is to induce run-holders to accept
longer tenure and other advantages in satisfaction
of the claims now accruing. “Remember,” says
Jenkin Coles, “ if the squatters accept the alterna-
tive it will only be because the State is getting the
worst of the bargain ! ” Which doesn’t follow.
Lots of bargains profit both parties.
The claim that the lunatic William Cres-
well is identical with the long-lost Roger Tich-
borne, is much discounted by the allegation of
Mrs. Jenkins, his “discoverer,” that she was
“impelled by a voice from the dead.” Spooks
are at a discount just now. And what matters it
intrinsically whether or not Creswell is Tichborne ?
Creswell is childless, and being a lunatic he can’t
make a will, and when he dies young Tichborne,
the present Bart., would succeed him.
In all English-manufactured maps of Aus-
tralia there appears a large circular blot of ink on
the Goulburn river, about 20 miles below Seymour,
denominated “ Mitchellstown,” which the average
Britisher sizes up as a town of equal
importance with Ballarat or Bendigo. The site
of this township was marked by Major Mitchell
in 1840 or thereabouts, but since then nothing
more has been heard about it. The map-draughts-
man, however, goes on making Hingland “Re-
member Mitchellstown” by dumping a yearly
increasing blot to indicate its position. It is now
really about time for some enterprising deacon to
go ’Ome and trade off a few magnificent Mitchells-
town city blocks on the British investor.
The White Horse Cellar Whisky is a
brand which has stood the test of more than 150
years and is still in as good repair as ever. The
world has progressed a lot in a century and a
half, but the art of whisky-making has evidently
stood still, for the White Horse Cellar brand is
still blended according to the same old recipe, and
no brand of later date can excel it. Evidently the
things that our drinky forefathers didn’t know
about whisky were not worth knowing. Langdon
and Co., of Melbourne and Clarence-street,
Sydney, and Harrold Bros, of Adelaide, are
wholesale agents for this venerable liquor, whose
age alone should commend it to the political and
other fossils of the land.
A few years ago, while searching the
archives of the Church of Sainte Croix of Bor-
deaux for the purpose of studying the old MSS.
relating to the history of the venerable abbey of
Sonlao, which had been built for a number of
centuries, a lucky chance led to the discovery of a
very old prescription of dentifrice, composed by
the Rev. Father Pierre Boursaud, Prior of the
monastery about the end of the 14th century.
It was found that the Rev. Father had bequeathed
to posterity an elixir of which the virtues are
simply marvellous ; and the most brilliant proofs
have shown that the preservation of the teeth is
not the only virtue that this precious specific
enjoys, but that its effects are most beneficial in
all that relates to the hygiene of the mouth. This
incomparable elixir prevents decay, and acts like a
tonic on the gums, which it hardens and hinders
from swelling, at the same time purifying and
sweetening the breath, leaving in the mouth a
delicious and lasting freshness, while it con-
tinuously opposes sore throat, hoarseness, ulcera-
tions, and inflammation of all kinds. In one
word, the daily use of the Elixir-Dentrifice of the
Reverend Benedictine Fathers of the Abbey of
Soulac assures the health of the mouth and
throat; and in preserving the teeth even of per-
sons of advanced age, renders easy the work of
the digestive organs by permitting thorough
mastication.
Sydney Wool Sales.
M‘Burney, Wallis & Co. report.—On the 18th instant
we placed before buyers a catalogue of some 2000 bales
wool, representing all the districts from which wool is
now arriving in Sydney. For free and light merino fleece
we found competition very strong, prices ruling up to late
rates. Crossbred is still receding in price, and, as we
wrote in our last Annual, the “ fad” for this class of wool
seems to be fast dying out. Scoured wool is still very
difficult to quit at reasonable prices. Greasy pieces and
bellies are still from lid. to 2d. per lb. lower than last
season. As buyers claim the privilege of a Christmas rest,
no sales will be held before January. On the 10th of that
month we will submit to purchasers another large and
attractive catalogue. We hope the new year may have
better times in store for the wool producers of Australia
and for trade in general. Sydney, December 21.
Art is struggling to secure a local habita-
tion and a name in Melbourne, and the Exhibition
trustees are doing all they can to foster the nurs-
ling in the southern city. In this connection,
attention may be directed to the enterprise which
the trustees have now in hand —an exhibition of
photographic works—landscapes, seascapes, photo-
gravures, autotypes, and, in fact, any illustration
of photography—which opens on 29th January,
prox., and continues to 30th April, prox. The
secretary, Mr. J. E. Sherrard, will furnish all in-
formation to intending exhibitors.

The Caligraph Writ tin Machine.
B
UAJMCKAPH embodies all the highest
i developments of applied mechanics, and its
principle having stood the test of 15 years, its
success towers above the fantastic devices of mush-
room competitors.
“ I like Caligraph writing better than that of
any other typewriter because the type comes out
so sharply and clearly.” The prominent Anglican
clergyman, who used these words, had hit upon
the chief characteristic of the Caligraph without
exactly knowing what brought it about. Tin-
result is attributable to the fact that the platen of
the Caligraph being bevelled the type strike
against a flat surface thus producing a full and
even impression. When you see typewriting with
only the middle of the letters clear you know that
the machine used was not a Caligraph, but om-
fitted witli the ordinary round platen. When you
read a typewritten letter, with some of the type
leaning to the right and the rest to the left, you
may be sure that your correspondent has a crude
machine, with curved typebars, which naturally
become more crooked, and thus twist their typ--
out of perpendicular. Then remember that the
Caligraph has straight typebars which, like every
thing else in the machine, are fitted with adjust-
able bearings.
The Caligraph manifolds 15 carbon copies at
one operation, and used with the Edison Mimeo-
graph reduplicates 1500 copies from one stencil.

All the big Melb. newspapers are about to
adopt type-setting machines. This will be par-
ticularly rough on the Melbi comps., as the closing-
up of the Standard ’ recently, and Melb. Daily
Telegraph three years ago, has filled the market
with surplus hands. The youth who is learning
to be a compositor nowadays might almost as well
be apprenticed to a crusader. He is learning a
trade which will be practically extinct just when
he is ready to make his living by it.
The deaths in Maoriland from cancer aver-
aged 5.02 per 10,000 living persons in ’93 ; in ’Bl, the
number per 10,000 was only 2.69 ! A West Coast
(South Island) stock-inspector triumphantly points
out that cancer has vanished from his district as a
result of his persistent destruction of every beast
showing the slightest trace of the disease. This
contrasts with a startling report from the Jeypore
hospital (Bengal, India), and one that has been a
hard nut for vegetarians to crack. Out of 102
major operations for cancer performed in the
Jeypore hospital, 61 were performed on strict
vegetarians, and only 41 on meat-eaters. Not-
withstanding which, Maoriland will be wise if it
acts upon its own experience. By the way, a N.S.
Wales J.P., remarkable for his execration of the
barmaid-system, seems to distinguish sharply
between moral and physical pests. He was fined
the other day for auctioning a. cancered bullock.
Dr. A. W. Momerie, the Broad Church
Christian and ex-Professor of Logic at London
University, now,on a visit to Australia, is a fine
example of “As the twig’s bent, the tree’s in-
clined/’ His criticism of ecclesiastical dogma is
singularly clear-headed and destructive. He is
the author of many crisp sentences like these :
The priests were the gentlemen who undertook to
square the a consideration.
The orthodox man prostitutes his conscience to save
his soul.
Morality without a God is infinitely better than a God
without morality. _
He says the Bible is not infallible; the doc-
trine of the atonement is grossly immoral;
the doctrines of the trinity and the resur-
rection are ridiculous. He declares that “what
usually passes current in the present day
under the name of Christianity is a compound
of pre-Christian paganism, post-Christian meta-
physics, and a little—a very little—of the actual
teachings of Christ Himself.” Yet he is still a
clergyman of the Established Church of England !
His early bias was towards the Church; and a fine
capacity for casuistry has enabled him hitherto, by
reconciling opposites, to evade complete intellectual
emancipation. “ Logic is logic,” as Dr. Holmes
remarked.
Victorian poultry, almost unsaleable in
Melbourne at 2s. a pair, lately realised in London
4s. a pair, whilst plumper and better birds —local
price, ■ 3s. a pair—brought in the British capital
ss. to ss. 3d. Thes.s. Ophir took away from Mel-
bourne last trip 1000 tons of butter, the largest
shipment on record.
A few days ago the cable announced that
the usual Anarchist bomb, with fuse attached, had
been discovered in London, and extinguished by a
gallant policeman just in time to avert the most
awful consequences. Next day the usual explana-
tion came along announcing that there was no
explosive in the “ bomb.” As a bomb consists of
any hollow, metallic substance capable of holding
anything that will explode, the chances are that
this one was a meat-tin or a saucepan. As for the
fuse, it was probably an effort of the policeman’s
imagination, or a lucifer match, or the reflection
bf a street lamp.
Lika-Joko Furniss is a nephew of the
Well-known Father Furniss, who wrote that blood-
curdling little book, “Hell open to Christians,”
which forms part of the pabulum offered to
juvenile R.C.’s, Furniss, by the way, has some-
how made himself much disliked by his press
Confreres, and there are not many lamentations to
be heard oveir the failure of his “comic” paper,
named after himself. Perhaps “failure” is too
strong a word, but a well-known man says “ it is
even worse than he hoped it would be.” The fact
is that when Furniss was ejected to the Garrick
he got too big for his boots, and rather cold-
shouldered some old friends.
A Sydney paper is reprinting the “spook’
expose by Dr. Knaggs, the well-known Sydney sur-
geon, of spiritualist Slade’s tricks as performed in
Sydney and Melbourne years ago. The doctor
unravelled the alleged mysteries of table-rapping,
slate writing and ambulatory chairs and tables, by
the agency of a strip of looking-glass between his
knees. Slade, by the way, told a Melbourne
journalist that he knew it was all up with him
when Knaggs came a second time to the seance.
Slade, at the time, was visiting Hyslop, the well-
known Melbourne bedding-manufacturer and
financier, with Junor-Brown (Alfred Deakin’s
father-indaw), of the old Melbourne spiritualistic
Lyceum gang. Hyslop, though one of the cult,
was one of the keenest and shrewdest business men
that ever resided in Melbourne. He followed Dr.
Knaggs’ method and completely demonstrated in
like manner that the only “spirit” brought to
bear by Slade was his extraordinarily flexible foot
which, encased in patent-leather shoes of glove-
like tenuity, enabled him to dispense with the use
of his hands in the evolution of his “ phenomena.”
But did this convert Hyslop ? Not a bit of it. It
didn’t even slacken him off Slade. His explana-
tion was that Slade, to provide against occasional
collapses of “dccult” power, had to resort to
physical ggency iq critical moments. No one can
remember how often spiritualism has been busted
up and exposed and utterly discredited, yet it
always starts again as fresh as ever.

Exhibition of works
Of the
PHOTOGRAPHIC ART,
To be Held in the
MELBOURNE EXHIBITION GALLERIES,
From 29th JANUARY till 30th APRIL, 1895.
The Exhibition Trustees have determined to hold an
Exhibition of the Photographic Art in the Galleries con-
nected with the Aquarium, and cordially invite the
assistance and co-operation of all those who are interested
in the Photographic Art.
The Exhibition will embrace all the various branches of
the art, and will include Portraiture, Landscape, and
Scenery subjects, together with specimens of Photo-
gravure, Autotype, and all the various processes of repro-
duction of pictures from photographs, together with an
exposition of the application of Photography to Art,
Industry, and Commerce.
A special section will be placed at the disposal of
amateurs for the exhibition of their work, and a separate
room will be placed at the disposal of the Amateur Photo-
graphic Association of Victoria.
The Exhibition will be open on 29th January, and
remain open till April 30, 1895. All exhibits must be
furnished with an explanatory label, legibly written or
printed.
The trustee* reserve the right to reject any proffered
exhibits, and to remove any objectionable labels, &c., if
necessary.
Each exhibitor of photographs, &0., of not less than
£lO in value will be furnished with a pass for entry during
the currency of the exhibition.
All exhibits must be delivered at the Exhibition-build-
ing not later than January 19, and placed as directed by
the trustees.
Entry forms and all further particulars can be obtained
on application to the undersigned.
J. E. SHERRARD,
Seeretary Exhibition Trustees.

The History of New South Wales,
From the Records :
Illustrated.
By Alexander Britton.
Edited by F. M. Bladen;- Barrister-at-La w.
In this Volume the History of New South Wales
is continued up to the year 1794. As in the case
of Vol. I, the materials from which the work has
been prepared comprise the letters and despatches
of the principal officers in charge at Sydney, and
a number of important State papers procured
from various sources in London and the Colony,
many of which have never before seen the light.
The work is profusely illustrated with maps and
authentic portraits of the principal men of the
period. Demy Bvo,, 354 pp.

Colony.]

r Index, an International Review of Insurance, Trade and Finance, writes as follows--respecting the
LIVERPOOL & LONDON & GLOBE
Total A ssets — INSURANCE COMPANY.
For years past sensationalism has been rampant in the fire insurance world ; and to find that a Company which
has steadily declined to d viate from the quiet far-seeing and self-respecting policy which has given .the Liverpool and
London aiid Globe its proud position, which has refused to be tempted into any lurid display of financial fireworks,
continues to so easily outdistance all its competitors, is a special pleasure and gratification to those'who wish to pre-
vent fire insurance from degenerating into a mad scramble for other people’s loaves and fishes, and \vho desire to see
the dignity of the profession worthily upheld. . . ..
The fire department for the past year shows an increase of premium income of over £43,000, and a trading account
su plus of £70,954, or about 4J per cent, of the premiums. The figures for three years will serve by way of com-

u
The other day the orthodox Anglicans of
Australia, by episcopal request, exercised “ self-
denial ” in order to aid the funds for foreign
missions. Putting the scheme in other words,
the heathen in Australia suffered, by “self-
denial,” for the benefit of heathen elsewhere
and of the travellers who live by them.
The Victorian Trades Defence Association,
it is reported, “assures Mr. Turner that a re-
duction in the import duties on spirits, wine and
beer would materially increase both the revenue
and the consumption.” In other words, it will
make the people so much more drunken than they
are, and will send so much more money out of the
country in payment for imported blue-ruin, that
the reduction in the duty will be more than com-
pensated for. Query : If it is proposed to
deliberately make the community drunk in order
to secure revenue, why not licence opium-dens,
lotteries, gambling-houses and houses of ill-fame,
and go in generally for making a revenue out of
sin in all its available forms ?
“ There is an ‘ unearned increment ’ when
the stock of some particular horse wins the Cup
and Derby—all other colts and fillies springing
from the same sire increase in value,” says Melb.
Aequs by way of demonstrating that unearned
increment in land values should not be annexed
by the State. The comparison is asinine. In the
case of the horse the increment is earned by the
animal himself, and the increase in the price of
his stock, if it occurs, is occasioned, not by his
winning the race, but because his success give-
proof that he has good material in him, and comes
of a good stock. When a piece of land in Collins-
street wins the Melbourne Cup the Argus’ horse
argument will begin to be reasonable.

(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Ycu of the Past, are you present?
Draw nearer, my heart is sore.
Was yours the fall of the foot in the hall ?
Was yours the face at the door?
As I lifted ray eyes I saw you ;
, You vanished and all was still;
And only outside the white owl cried,
And the moon stared over the hill.
Wan-blue were your eyes, O Shadow,
And paler your aspect than seems
The mystical star that glimmers afar
In a land of mysterious dreams.
O, Shadow, the past is present,
And empty your coffin and tomb;
Draw near, draw near, chill child of fear,
; From the frontier-land of Gloom.
When you were a girl, grey Shadow,
And life in these veins ran red,
My heart went out to your golden mouth
And the curls about your head.
Did you know that I loved you, Shadow?
Did you guess whence the violets came?
And the delicate heart with its Cupid dart,
All opal and ruby-flame ?
Those days, brown gold were the lashes
That shadowed your dreaming eyes,
4-nd your teeth were pearl ’neath the coral curl
Of twin portals of Paradise.
And redder your cheeks were and softer
(Alas, they are pale and cold !)
Than the rose of the East, or the wine that the
priest
Drinks out of his carven gold.
It was all so sad, O Shadow,
And you faded away so soon,
Like a note that flies and fades and dies
Ere it grows to a golden tune.
Gone ! utterly gone, O Shadow,
No whisper, no word let fall;
No light is shed, and the moon is dead,
And a chill creeps up the wall.
I shall follow and follow you, Shadow,
Till the sun, .remote and red,
Burns like a spark, and dim and dark
Rise up the hosts of dead.
RODERIC QUINN.
Philosophy has never given a good reason
why a man, who, at 25, is willing to share with
the world, at 55, will shoot down the first who
wants to borrow a match from him.
Several N.S.W. public - school teachers
have of late been approached with offers of liberal
remuneration if they will incorporate in the home
exercises of their pupils favourable comments on
certain brands of newly - introduced domestic
necessaries. In one case the agent of a firm
agreed to pay cash down on examining the exercise
books at the end of each month. The great art of
advertising again.
The Westraliau bonanza-men are decorat-
ing themselves in the East. One Londonderry
potentate has bought “ the biggest (yellow)
diamond in Sydney ” for .£llO, and when he
showed it to a mate, in presence of a friend, the
mate said : “ I’ve got one, too ” indicating a large
wart on the middle joint of his little finger—“the
garotters can have that if they like.” And con-
cerning the bejewelled glory of a Wealth of
Nations magnate—you should just see him when
he’s got ’em all on.
The sapient S.M. Herald gravely records
how R. E. O’Connor was introduced to Sir Oliver
Morton (presumably Mowat), Premier of Toronto
(Ontario). Which is the same as if a Toronto paper
had remarked that Mackenzie Bowell was intro-
duced to “Mr. George Re Ige, Premier of Sydney.”
Next day’s Sydney Telegraph repeated the
blunder. Yet the Sydney papers are never tired
of commenting on the ignorance of Australian
localities displayed by foreign critics. But it will
be a cold day when the physician heals himself.
Municipal grovel, even in London, has no
ancient precedents to justify its modern awfulness.
A century ago the Lord Mayor disputed the
Pripee of Wales’claim to rank before him within
the city bounds, and the King decided in favor of
the angry soap-boiler, otherwise the soap-boiler
would have made things darned unpleasant for
His Majesty. Nowadays, the chief business of a
Lord Mayor is to lick Albert Edward’s boots on
behalf of the “loyal citizens,” and wink at an all-
round maladministration of charity trusts and
guild moneys.

When one sets a candle in a window on a dark
night he never can tell how many lost and be-
wildered travellers it may guide on their way.
I once knew a dear lady who habitually did it
on every dark night. The road that passed her
house was always forsaken and lonesome enough,
and houses were far apart. So, on the general
principle of goodwill to men, she placed her
candle in a sort of projecting window, where it
would shine both ways and do the most good.
She is dead and gone now, but let us hope that
her family keeps up the custom. To be sure, it
brought her many a strange guest, yet she did
what she could for them, and never grumbled—
pay or no pay.
A year or two ago a little book was printed,
containing an account, by Mr. John Hodson, of
Warboys, Hunts, of the way he was cured of a
wasting disease by Mother Seigel’s Curative
Syrup—-a complaint contracted in India, where
Mr. Hodson was once a soldier in the British
Army.
This candle of Hodson’s beamed hopefully in
all directions, and, among others, its rays fell
upon the eyes of a woman who needed light at
that particular time. How it came to pass she
tells in a letter, from which we quote the follow-
ing:—
“I was,” she says, “always healthy up to
May, 1884, when I nad an attack of gastric
fever, which left me low and feeble. I had a
bad taste in the mouth, and after everything I
ate I had great pain in the chest and sides, and
also dreadful pam at the back and between the
shoulder-blades, and a sinking feeling at the pit
of the stomach. My legs trembled and shook
under me, so I could not walk out. Indeed, it
was as much as I could do to get across the
floor. A dry, hacking cough set in and shook
me very much, and I lost a deal of sleep.
“As I grew weaker I was confined to my bed
more and more, and my daughter was obliged to
take my place in doing the housework. A doctor
attended me for over a year. He tried first one
kind of medicine and then another, but none of
them helped me. At the end of the year the
doctor said he could do no more for me, and
recommended me, as a last resort, to try a change
of air.
“I had got a despair of ever getting better
when a book was sent to me full of statements
from different people, telling how they had been
made well of various complaints by using Mother
Seigel’s Curative Syrup, and I read of a young
man living at Warboys, near me, having been
cured in a marvellous manner by this medicine.
I got a bottle from Messrs. Palmer and Sons,
chemists, Ramsey, and, after I had taken it a
week, I felt relief and gained strength. All my
vain left me, and I have never looked behind me
since. I take an occasional dose of the Syrup
and keep in excellent health. I give you full
permission to use this letter as you think fit.
Yours truly, (signed) Sarah Mason, Ramsey
Heights, Ramsey, Hunts, January 27th, 1892.”
What better use could we possibly put this
lady’s letter to than to publish it ? In this way
it has fallen into the present reader’s hands, and
may turn out to be a candle in the window to
show him refuge and shelter beside some long and
floomy path. A word more on this very point,
t is not a matter of choice whether we are to
help one another. We are obliged and bound to do
so by every consideration of morality and utility.
Humanity is like the body of one man : if a single
part is neglected, the rest must pay the penalty.
Therefore, set candles in windows. If, for
instance, Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup has
done you good, say so, publish it, make it known.
Others near you may be groping in darkness.
And it is afl the more a subject of rejoicing that
this remedy, as in the above case, conquers indi-
gestion and dyspepsia, because that complaint it
is which clouds the aim for millions on Life’s
hard road.

of the Fattest.

A Queensland station has a lovely liar.
He lately entered an up-country store and asked
for strychnine. “ Dingoes thick! ’’ he said.
“ Why, I woke up t’other night and looked out
and 1 saw ’em just like a mob o’ sheep. I’ll
take half-an-ounce of the stuff to make ’em sit
up.” Now, that storekeeper was out of strychnine,
but rather than lose his customer he substituted
Epsom salts. A week later the purchaser re-
turned. “ Well, how did you get on with the
dingoes?” enquired the storekeeper. “Found 28
of them dead next morning. Haven’t seen one
since.’’
A statement of the service of Col. Charles
Holled Smith, the new Yic. Commandant :
Joined British Army in ’65; served in Zulu and
Boer Wars, ’79 to ’81; Egypt, ’B2; Tel-el-Kebir,
and Nile Expeditions, ’B4-5; Soudan Frontier
Force, ’B5 7 ; Gov.-General of Red Sea Littoral,
’BB-’92. Apparently Australia will never get rid
of the odour of Zulu and Soudani massacres. If
Australia ever has to defend itself it will be
against a civilised European enemy, and experience
gained in fighting badly-armed and undisciplined
savages will be useless. Wellington, when he
left India, entirely changed his system of tactics.
At Argaum and Assays he attacked the Mah-
rattas with all his force. In Spain and at Water-
loo he always stood on the defensive, and awaited
attack. Both plans succeeded because they were
suited to the conditions. But then every Soudan
colonel has not Wellington’s genius.
Austria has decided to tax cats. There
was nothing else in the whole empire that hadn’t
a duty on it —even chickens, geese, goats, and hogs
have been poll-taxed for many years past—and
now the cat goes the way of the rest and a beauti-
ful uniformity prevails. Austria, with its poor
soil, its eighteen or twenty separate tribes, most
of them hating each other, and speaking languages
which none of the others understands, its huge
debt, the vast army under which it has to stagger
in order to avoid being wiped out by its aggressive
neighbors, and its taxation system under which
it is hardly possible to fall down in the street
without paying a duty, is a wonderful example of
Thrift under the hardest conditions that can well
be imagined. The Australian Fat Man, wailing
over the prospective land-tax, should take a look
at this hard-driven community and shudder as he
realises what he may yet come to.
Dear Bulletin, —Much unfavorable com-
ment has been made on what is termed the dis-
proportion in the numbers of crew and passengers
saved from the Wairarapa. Perhaps the follow-
ing may be some explanation—l don’t say it is so.
The great bulk of passengers on an intercolonial
boat belong to the commercial class. When the
maritime strike occurred it was this class which
most strenuously opposed the endeavor of the
seamen to better themselves. Merchants, store-
keepers and clerks went out of their way to
leave their own employment to load and un-
load the ships. A seaman on strike is re-
garded as a public enemy whom it is the duty
of the whole community to oppose and defeat.
When a wreck occurs the same men are expected
to risk their own lives in the endeavor to provide
for the safety of those who have done their utmost
to deprive them of their livelihood —to see that all
the passengers are safely bestowed before they
venture to secure for themselve* a lifebelt or a
place in a boat. Jack, Oscar and Hans have hearts
as good and brave as anyone, but they have
memories also. —Yours, &c., Waihoa.

Fitzgeralds’ Circus was, at latest dates, painting the
town of Wellington red—not forgetting blue, green and
yellow. A little tiger has been born into the Fitz
menagerie since it reached Maoriland. Two or three
little tigers were born, no doubt, but only one of them
survives. The others died naturally or were swallowed
by their doting mother when she caught brother Tom
staring at them. 0 for a way of inducing a wife to gulp
down the haby!
The bar of a Melb. theatre was the scene the other night
of a vigorous fist display between a popular baritone and
an inferior light playing in burlesque in another theatre.
The former, being muscular, wiped his smaller opponent
out in one act, and the latter’s face was closed for three
days for alterations and repairs.
The committee of the Distressed Actors’ Fund, Melb.,
advertises th.it after providing for the inmates of the
Home, a sum of money remains for outside distribution,
and invites members of the “ profesh ” who may be in
distress to apply for relief. Judging from the number
of mummers in Melb. who are down on their luck, the
< '“h held by the committee is as good as swamped fifty
times over.'

A Miss Hope Booth recently brought a much-boomed
variety co. from Amurrika to London, and established a
record there by the velocity of her burst. She opened on
Thursday night to a thin audience. On Friday she
opened to no audience at all, and on Saturday morning
the show disbanded.
Haddon Chambers’ new play, “ John-a-Dreatna ” (writes
The Bulletin’s London correspondent), was produced on
November 9 at the Haymarket Theatre before an enthu-
siastic audience, the author receiving an unusually flatter-
ing ovation at the conclusion of the piece. It would be
rash to hastily express an opinion as to whether it is a
great play, or one which will stand the test of time—one
must see it two or three times before coming to a con-
clusion 6n these points—but, anyhow, it is a distinct
advance upon anything Chambers has done before, and
leaves “The Idler” far behind. So far, the critics are
unanimous in their approval. The play hinges upon a
very charming young woman, Kate Cloud—admirably re-
presented by Mrs. Patrick Campbell—who is presumably
the daughter of a “fallen woman,” and who, when her
mother comes upon bad times, sacrifices her virtue in
order to maintain her parent (this has been done before,
and well done, too, in Murray’s novel, “ A Song of Six-
pence This much we glean from a sort of confession
she makes to a very nice old country parson (Nutcombe
Gould). She is, in fact, a kind ©f alleviated “Second
Mrs. Tanqueray,” or, as the author euphemistically puts I
it, “ the hem of her garment is frayed.” Fortunately she j
comes under the influence of an old, benevolent lady—
who is not introduced to us, for the reason that
she is presumably dead—but who apparently does
rescue work in some great city, and has the good
fortune to tear Kate from her evil surroundings.
Later she finds herself on board a yacht in the Mediter-
ranean, as the guest of Lord and Lady Barbridge, where
Sir Hubert Garlinge (Charles Cartwright), makes violent
love to Kate, who cannot reciprocate his passion, for the
reason that there is on board, at the same time, one
Harold Wynn, called by his intimates John A’Dreams,
from his devotion to the stars and waves, about which he
writes uncommonly bad poetry. This gentleman (repre-
sented by Beerbohm Tree) has also fallen in love with Kate,
and upon these characters and their doings and comings
and goings the whole plot is worked, and worked for all
it is worth. Kate makes a confident of the old parson,
who is the father of Harold, carefully pumps the old
gentleman as to whether she is fit to marry an honour-
able man, and, having received an answer in the affirma-
tive, she tells him that the man who loves and wishes
to marry her is his son. The obvious horror of the
serious old preacher makes her at once determine
that she is no fit mate for a good man, and she
bids her lover good-bye for ever. Very soon after she
repents her decision and comes back and offers herself to
him “body and soul.” Garlinge overhears her, deter-
mines to make her his, and gives Harold a big dose of
opium, which puts him to sleep for 20 hours, at the end
of which period he awakes and proceeds, among a large
number of thrilling circumstances, to rescue his Kate
from a decidedly painful situation on board a yacht. The
aforesaid Cartwright, by the way, has bought the rights
of “ John-a-Dreams” for Australia. Haddon Chambers
has been reproached for his lack of capacity to write
comedy, but this charge can be no longer levelled against
him, as there is some admirable fun made by certain minor
characters.
Probable cast for “ Aladdin ”at Melb. Royal: Aladdin,

Roberts ; Washee Washee, Robert Baxter ; Emperor, Olly

Fitz Gibbon, the White Knight of Melbourne, who
travels on his inhuman dignity, never misses a show in
which any reference, however puerile, is made to his
almighty self. He books a front seat in the circle, and
lurks there till the allusion comes on; then he swells
visibly, and beams upon the audience. The most horrible
pun upon his name is incense to Fitz.—a tribute to his
grandeur. Dignity is usually vanity in the form of a
disease. y
Circus-man Abell’s financial embarrassment is only
temporary. He invested a lot of money in real estate at
Singapore, on the off-chance of Australia proving a duffer.
May the old man yet prosper 1
More journalistie enterprise. On the day that the
Melb. Velodrome opened, the then-living Standard came
out at 2 p.m. with an account of the ceremony and the
speech of Zox, M.L.A., reported in full. Yet the real
opening did Hot take place until an hour later, and Zox
faiUd to attend at all.
They all do it. Signora Duse is divorcing her husband.
But it is natural enough—no one absolutely devoted to
his or her art can afford to marry. It is like committing
bigamy.

When Minnie Hauk was playing “ Carmen ” at’Frisco
she apparently got mashed on Ravelli, the tenor, and one
night, when he was in the middle of a high note, she
embraced him so suddenly and violently that his waist-
coat was torn almost in two pieces in his efforts to
escape. Next ight, Minnie’s husband hovered round in
the wings wilh a revolver, announcing his resolve to
shoot Ravelli if there was any more embracing ; and the
miserable tenor, realising that his life was in danger,
armed himself with a carving-knife with which to stave
off Minnie if she became too affectionate. Every time
Minnie approached hi r stage lovi r fled wildly across the
boards to avoid her, announcing in song, as he did so,
how he longed to take her to his arms, while Minnie
trotted after him in search of another hug.

MISS FO-FUM.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
It was a big yellow flower-pot that intro-
duced me to her—whirling perilously close to my
astonished nose before it smashed upon the cobble-
stones within 6ft. of me.
I looked up— and also, 1 suppose, looked foolish—
for she laughed until the pudding-faced lantern-
maker across the way waddled out with a fret-
saw in his hand and twinkling curiosity in the
little black eyes that were as currants unto the
pudding aforesaid.
“ Confound your notion of a joke ! ” I muttered,
with a growl that somehow twisted itself into a
laugh as I picked up a fragment of the pot to
pretend retaliation. Then I stood astonished ; for
the girl—a Chinese girl, on the porch-top of a
Chinese house—called out in unexceptionable
English—“ I’m sure I beg your pardon ! I didn’t
see you, you know. Do forgive me ! ”
I looked again. There was no doubt of it;
from her flower-decked ebony hair to her comical
little clumpy slippers, there was nothing un-
Chinese about her but her speech. The flower- pot,
I readily understood, was one of those which —
placed empty and mouth-outwards on the porch of
a Chinese house—proclaimed to all Mongolian
bachelors the husbandless condition of some slant-
eyed damsel within. And this English-speaking
virgo celestialis—h&xng doubtless a lady of superior
views—had, thus, with scorn and energy, cast
forth the symbolical earthenware.
And was this her father—this lean and leathery
pagan, beckoning to me from the door, and ex-
plaining to me, in that Canton “ pidgin ” which
it were distraction-to reproduce, that his daughter,
who was “all same English lady,” would be glad
to make for me tea and further apologies.
* » •
“In Calcutta,” she said. “I was eight
years there, and I had been at an English school
in Canton before that. But I am, in everything
else; even” —twinkling roguishly at me over a
miraculously-carved fan—“ even in religion !—a
Chinawoman altogether.”
“In which religion, may I ask ? There are three
in China, you know ! ”
“ And three hundred in England ! ” she
answered quickly. “ But I meant Buddhism.”
“ SVell, Buddhism’s as good a creed as any
other—and as bad ! ”
She held up a little lemon-tinted finger—not,
thank heaven ! adorned with a three-inch claw.
“ You don’t talk like a Christian,” she said.
“It would be strange if I did ! But, surely,
they tried to make one of you at your English
schools ? ”
She laughed till her gold tunic-links tinkled.
“ Oh, yes! —and they succeeded, too ! —as much as
they ever do—these wise teachers of yours, who
think that when they’ve got the shell they’ve got
the kernel! ”
In a dazed kind of way I looked round the
room. Grotesquely-twisted furniture ; ferociously
pictured screens; fantastically gorgeous porcelain
—Chinese enough, all this; and yet—this girl’s
talk—
“ Now, don’t look so astonished ! Don b your
own women think for themselves?”
“ They’re telling us very hard that they do,
just now, at all events. But I never heard one of
them take your view of the proselytising business.”
“ Do you take it ?”
“Oh, yes ! It’s a common enough view amongst
men.”
“ Christian men ! ” —and this extraordinary
young person laughed again till shespilt some of the
tea she was handing me. “ Do you know it seems
to me that the majority of Christian men are
simply Pagans at heart!”
“ Our own women have begun to tell us so,” I
said, getting up to go. “But they mean it for a
reproach. Do you?”
She put out her little yellow hand to be shaken.
“I mean it for the truth !” she said.
* * *
For a week I had matters in hand which
kept me from thinking much about my remark-
able little heathen, but, just as I was projecting
another call at the mcwson-Fo-Fum, I dropped
across her under extraordinary circumstances.
The gilded ball on the Van den Bosch monu-
ment was shining with the last of the sun as I
came towards it from the arsenal. Near its base
I saw two Dutch soldiers and a group of native
women whom I took to be of a class much
encouraged by the Dutch Government’s unwilling-
ness to let its warriors bring wives from Europe.
They were jabbering excitedly round some object
which I could not see, until the centre —breaking
suddenly through the circumference—came run-
ning towards me, for it was a woman.
“ A Chinawoman ! ” I said, as she came.
“Great Scott! it’s Miss Fo-Fum ! ”
Her hair, like a black horse’s tail, was stream-
ing over a tunic rent nearly from her back ; her
face was scratched and rand-stained ; she had lost
one of her slippers.
The Javanese women, cackling and squealing
behind her, pulled up at sightof a white man, and
scurried back after the two uniformed heroes, who
were not too drunk to be already making an un-
ostentatious departure.
“Don’t tell me any thing till you’ve recovered your
breath,” I said to the girl, who was twisting up
her hair and trying to reduce the gaps in her silk
tunic, the gold links of which, I noticed, had been
nearly all wrenched away. v
“See !” I said—“you have been robbed, also.”
“I have been robbed of ray secret!” she said,
angrily. “No —you must not see me home!
Neither must you come there any more !”
“ But ”
“ No ‘ huts,’sir ! Is it because I am a China-
woman that you would force yourself on me?”
She moved away a dozen yards—then came
suddenly back.
“Did you hear what those Javani beasts were
calling me? ” she asked.
“I understand very little Javanese ; none at all
of that kind ! I only noticed that one word was
often repeated. Something like ”
With a short scream—the only hysterical symp-
tom she had shown—she threw up her arms, and
rushed away into the dusk.
I stood, puzzled, where she had left me.
“ Whatever your mystery may be, young
woman,” I said, “it’s plain you’re a bit of a
Tartar! ”
• * •
The man looked away the moment he
caught my eye; but he was too late; I had
recognised him as one of the soldiers I had seen
with the Javanese women, a couple of nights
before—the more certainly that he rejoiced in the
double distinction of being pock-pitted and cross-
eyed. In-uad-out he. dodged me amongst the
bullock-carts and vegetable-stalls, till —finding he
couldn’t shake me off—he pulled up under a fish-
monger’s awning, and pretended immense interest
in a huge leopard-fish suspended by the door.
Next, of course, he wouldn’t understand my limp-
ing Dutch, until a small transaction in silver
made me vastly more intelligible, and elicited the
fact that he understood Malay, to boot.
“For,” said he, in answer to my questions, “ if
mynheer knows what Kusali means, he knows
what those yellow sluts were calling after t()e
Chinawoman, in their own lingo.”
“Good God, man! But, why to her?—She’s
no leper!”
“Her mother is, though !—an old woman living
in a hut down by the mangrove-flats. And this
one goes to visit her. And these Javani trollops
watched her, and followed her. And that’s all I
know, mynheer !”
“One tiling more!” I said, as,he saluted and
was right-about-facing for the nearest arrack-shop.
“If these wretches thought the girl had the
disease, they wouldn’t have gone so near her,
surely ?”
“They don’t think it, mynheer. It’s only spite,
because she’s a Chinawoman, and has money and
fine clothes. All the same, I don’t go near those
cursed yellow cats any more ; and I would take
the liberty of advising mynheer to keep away from
that girl’s house.”
“Do you think the disease is communicable,
then ? Your own regimental surgeon says it
isn’t.”
“ I know he does : and he’s been a whole six
months out from Europe. But I’ve been fiye-
and-twenty years in the East, and I say it’s
catching. And Notaf. Raud says so, too ! ”
Notaf Raud I had heard much of as a native
physician, and to him I went, tor I couldn’t help
being strongly interested in this extraordinary
girl, with her strong sense and sharply-cut indivi-
duality—her curious hybridity between Western
progressivism and Oriental immobility.
The Javanese oracle—a paunchy little pundit in
silver-rimmed glasses—smiled at my question.
“ Truly you Europeans are a strange people,”
he said. “ You are a thousand times more afraid
of catching a disease that takes many years to kill
than you are of contracting—say, cholera —which
may bring death in a few hours !”
I wasn’t going to wrestle with the Oriental
mind on this point—“ Would my learned friend
kindly adhere to the question ?”
My “ learned friend,” betaking himself unto the
Dutch tongue, became briefly explicit,
“ Leprosy is communicable ; certainly by con-
tagion—and possibly, also, by infection. But it is
not communicable to all persons. There is special
predisposition in certain individuals as in certain
races. Is mynheer scientifically interested in the
question ? If so, I can show him some beautiful
cases!”
“ Beautiful cases !” —the very jargon of the
European professional ghoul ! I told the man I
had seen cases enough, and went away painfully
endeavouring to prevent my ghastly recollections
thereof from connecting themselves with thoughts
of my bright little pagan.
* * *
On a Dutch-India steamer, eight months
afterwards, I met a Sourabaya-man I knew.
“Do you remember the little Chinawoman I
used to tell you about? ” I said.
“ Oh, yes ! Let’s see ! —what was it you used
to call her ? Miss ”
“ Miss Fo-Fum 1 ”
“To be sure! Yes! Poor thing I—poor
thing ! ”
“ Worse ! Sent to Garka ! ”
“Garka?”
“ Ay ! the new establishment for lepers 1 ”
ALEX. MONTGOMERY.
Dear Bulletin, —In re laughing-jackass
discussion. As “Wurry M.” remarks, he kills
chickens. He also destroys eggs. He is also a
great fish-eater, a fact not generally known.
With regard to his alleged usefulness, he un-
doubtedly kills an occasional snake —in which
sphere of activity an ordinary bush cafe (which is
not protected) will beat him hollow. At no very
distant date many varieties of Australian snakes
will become extinct, and the jackass will continue
to ravage his feathered co-mates. In the interests
of Australian birds no special protection should be
afforded that wholesale robber and murderer—the
goburra.—P.L.
In the October Idler, Jerome K. Jerome
floats himself and four others into a co. to work
off an Australian story. Jerome does the first
chapter, introducing as his chief character the
Bar-keeper. “ Bar-keeper ”is the name given in
Australia to the man who runs a bush shanty.
When said bar-keeper rides abroad he hears the
whispering of the pines. “Pines” compose the
Australian “ forest. ” Jerome’s bar-keeper kills a
sick man for bis belt of gold, and then one Eden
Phillpotts takes up the tired tale. The pre-
dominant note of this chapter is that the local
inspector of police (sergeants do not have districts
in Australia) is “’Spec.” Eden’s contribution to
geography is “Flying Fox Corner.” When E. F.
Bensen comes along he exhibits his specialty,
which is Australian conversation. This is the way
his Australian actress (and every real specimen)
speaks :
“ I’ll be stepping down.” “ The trial will now be com-
mencing.” “Don’t be scatterin’ inspersions over others.”
Her habit is to drop the g’s, but sometimes she
drops the habit. When they give the lady a
drink, she says :—-
“ Who stands treat?” (Australian slang).
And the bar-keeper replies :
“The loss is the bank’s.” (An expression borrowed
from the Australian national game of Faro).
F. Frankfort Moore imports Choke-neck Gulch
into the 4th chapter. “ Choke-neck Gulch ”is the
resort of criminals. Australian criminals establish
exclusive colonies in “ gulches,” where the police
don’t disturb them. In the last chapter, Barry
Pain has to find a lost diamond. He makes a
man disembowel his dead brother with a clasp-
knife. The dead brother had swallowed the dia-
mond. Choke-neck Gulch 1 Choke-neck Gulch !

H. PATESON
“Every man can write at least one book,” every
man with brains has at least one good story to
tell; every man with or without brains moves in
a different circle and knows things unknown to
any other man. Write carefully and plainly on
one side of the paper only, obliterating every un-
necessary word; then mail your work to The
Bulletin, which pays for accepted matter. In
certain of the so-called prize-competitions the
great prizes fall to the newspaper-proprietor, who
gets the guileless public to work for him for noth-
ing. Short stories, or ballads, especially on bush,
mining, sporting, social, or dramatic themes, are
preferred by The Bulletin ; 1360 words go to a
column. If you can possibly keep your story
within a column all the better , Don’t write a
column on any subject if a half-column will do ;
don’t write half-a-column where a mere paragraph
is enough. “ Boil it down.”
Suggestions for illustration with or without
rough explanatory drawings will be gladly re-
ceived bv The Bulletin.
